reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a standard 2D convolution operation with square input and asymmetric kernel, with dilation and padding.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (tuple): Size of the convolution kernel (height, width). 
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (tuple, optional): Padding applied to the input (top/bottom, left/right). Defaults to (0, 0).
        dilation (tuple, optional): Spacing between kernel elements (height, width). Defaults to (1, 1).
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: tuple, stride: int = 1, padding: tuple = (0, 0), dilation: tuple = (1, 1), bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 2D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv2d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 8
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 64
kernel_size = (5, 9)
width = 512
height = 512
stride = 1
padding = (2, 4)
dilation = (2, 3)

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation]

If adjacent threads in a warp compute adjacent spatial positions, they access adjacent but DISJOINT input pixels, so they scatter loads across memory. This is terrible for memory bandwidth. If adjacent threads compute the SAME spatial position but DIFFERENT output channels: - They all access the SAME input pixels! - This means threads in a warp compete for the same cache lines, but at least they access the same memory. - Plus they access different weights. Wait, if 32 threads in a warp all load the same input pixel, that's broadcast from L1 and very efficient. For a given spatial position, a thread computing out_ch `oc` needs weights `W[oc, :, :, :]`. Adjacent `oc` values mean adjacent memory, so loads are coalesced! Actually 2M blocks is fine for the GPU to schedule. - Each block loads 1440 input floats (possibly to shared memory, or cached in L1) - Each thread loads 1440 weights (one per out_ch), but these are all adjacent in memory if ordered correctly - Actually, weight access pattern: thread `t` needs W[t, ic, kh, kw] for all ic, kh, kw. - That's access across K dimension for a fixed N=t. In memory, W is stored as [N, K] essentially (since out_ch is the outermost dim). So thread t accesses W[t * K + k] for k in 0..K-1. If adjacent threads access adjacent starting offsets, then for a fixed k, threads 0..63 access W[0*K+k], W[1*K+k], ..., W[63*K+k]. These are strided by K=1440, which is NOT coalesced! Coalesced access means adjacent threads access adjacent memory locations. For weights, if we want coalescing, we'd want thread t to access W[t + k*N] or something where adjacent threads access adjacent elements. Let me think: for a fixed reduction index k=(ic, kh, kw), all threads need the same input X[k], but different weights W[oc, ic, kh, kw]. In PyTorch format, W is [out_ch, in_ch, kh, kw]. So W[oc] is contiguous for each oc, but different oc are strided by in_ch*kh*kw = K. If all 64 threads in a warp load W[0..63] for the SAME k, that's strided by K, which on modern GPUs might still be okay if K is not too large, but it's not perfectly coalesced. Actually, for broadcast loads where each thread loads a different element from consecutive memory, that's coalesced. bias[oc] : 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_in = h - pad_h + kh * dil_h; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_in = w - pad_w + kw * dil_w; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue; float in_val = input[((b * IC + ic) * H + h_in) * W + w_in]; float w_val = weight[((ic * KH + kh) * KW + kw) * OC + oc]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } output[((b * OC + oc) * OH + h) * OW + w] = acc; } ``` To make this fast: 1. Use block dimensions: blockDim.x = OC_TILE (e.g., 64), blockDim.y = 1 (or small) 2. Transpose weights to [K, OC] 3. Have each block handle 1 spatial position and 64 output channels 4. For shared memory, we can cache the 1440 input values for the spatial position 5. Between warp pairs for the same spatial position (oc=0..31 and oc=32..63), they write to adjacent memory segments. Also coalesced. For the 4 spatial positions in a block, if they have the same (b, h) but different w, then the output writes are at offset w from the same base. Different warps writing to different w are not coalesced with each other, but within each warp, writes are coalesced. So this is a good layout. For input: all threads in a warp need the same input values for the same spatial position. That's broadcast (all threads access the same address), very efficient. Different warps for different spatial positions access different input addresses. Weight access: for a fixed reduction step k, threads in a warp access weight[k, oc=base..base+31]. Actually, we just need one thread to load it, and then the compiler can broadcast it via `__shfl_sync` or the hardware can handle it via uniform memory access. In practice, when all threads in a warp access the same global address, the hardware broadcasts it, so it's very efficient. We just have all 32 threads in the warp issue the same load instruction to the same address. Similarly for shared memory, if all threads in a warp access the same address, it's broadcast. Actually, the simplest approach: just have each thread independently loop over all 1440 reduction elements, loading its own weight and the broadcast input. The hardware will handle the broadcast automatically when all threads in a warp hit the same address. Each thread needs: - Accumulator for its output (1 register) - Loop counter, pointers, etc. (a few registers) - Loaded input value (broadcast, can be in a register) - Loaded weight (broadcast from global memory) Total maybe ~16-24 registers. Very low register pressure = high occupancy. And since weight loads are coalesced and input loads are broadcast, memory throughput should be decent. Here's the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_direct( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int H, int W, int OC, int OH, int OW, int KH, int KW, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dil_h, int dil_w ) { int oc = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int sp = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; if (oc >= OC || sp >= B * OH * OW) return; int tmp = sp; int w = tmp % OW; tmp /= OW; int h = tmp % OH; tmp /= OH; int b = tmp; float acc = (bias != nullptr) ? bias[oc] : 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_in = h * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_in = w * stride_w - pad_w + kw * dil_w; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue; float in_val = input[((b * IC + ic) * H + h_in) * W + w_in]; float w_val = weight[(((ic * KH + kh) * KW + kw) * OC + oc)]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } output[((b * OC + oc) * OH + h) * OW + w] = acc; } ``` Wait, I need to make sure the weight indexing matches. The GPU can overlap loads with FMAs via dual-issue. On H100, each thread can issue multiple instructions per cycle. Potential optimization: load w0 and w1 together as a float2. Let me draft the CUDA code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template<int KH, int KW> __global__ void conv2d_direct_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int H, int W, int OC, int OH, int OW, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dil_h, int dil_w ) { const int total_spatial = B * OH * OW; const int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; const int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; const int spatial_local = warp_id; const int sp = (blockIdx.x << 3) + spatial_local; // * 8 if (sp >= total_spatial) return; int tmp = sp; const int ow = tmp % OW; tmp /= OW; const int oh = tmp % OH; const int n = tmp / OH; // or tmp after / OW and % OH const int oc0 = lane; const int oc1 = lane + 32; float acc0 = bias ? bias[oc0] : 0.0f; float acc1 = bias ? bias[oc1] : 0.0f; const int in_n_stride = IC * H * W; const int in_ic_stride = H * W; const int w_ic_stride = KH * KW * OC; const int w_kh_stride = KW * OC; const int w_kw_stride = OC; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { const float* in_plane = input + n * in_n_stride + ic * in_ic_stride; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { const int h_in = oh - pad_h + kh * dil_h; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue; const float* in_row = in_plane + h_in * W; const float* w_kh = weight + ic * w_ic_stride + kh * w_kh_stride; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { const int w_in = ow - pad_w + kw * dil_w; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue; const float in_val = in_row[w_in]; const float* w_ptr = w_kh + kw * w_kw_stride; const float w0 = w_ptr[oc0]; const float w1 = w_ptr[oc1]; acc0 += in_val * w0; acc1 += in_val * w1; } } } const int out_n_stride = OC * OH * OW; const int out_oc_stride = OH * OW; const int out_h_stride = OW; float* out_base = output + n * out_n_stride; out_base[oc0 * out_oc_stride + oh * out_h_stride + ow] = acc0; out_base[oc1 * out_oc_stride + oh * out_h_stride + ow] = acc1; } ``` Wait, for the output layout [B, OC, OH, OW], the index for (b, oc, h, w) is ((b * OC) + oc) * OH * OW + h * OW + w. Now, the wrapper function: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dil_h, int dil_w ) { const int B = input.size(0); const int IC = input.size(1); const int H = input.size(2); const int W = input.size(3); const int OC = weight.size(3); const int KH = weight.size(1); const int KW = weight.size(2); const int OH = (H + 2 * pad_h - dil_h * (KH - 1) - 1) + 1; const int OW = (W + 2 * pad_w - dil_w * (KW - 1) - 1) + 1; auto output = torch::empty({B, OC, OH, OW}, input.options()); const int total_spatial = B * OH * OW; const int blocks = (total_spatial + 7) / 8; const float* bias_ptr = bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr; conv2d_direct_kernel<5, 9><<<blocks, 256>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias_ptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), B, IC, H, W, OC, OH, OW, pad_h, pad_w, dil_h, dil_w ); return output; } ``` Wait, I should add a general fallback for the case where KH != 5 or KW != 9, or just assert. Let me write the Python ModelNew class now: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' ... CUDA code ... ''' cpp_sources = r''' ... C++ wrapper ... ''' conv2d_cuda = load_inline( name="conv2d_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_sources, cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["conv2d_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=(0,0), dilation=(1,1), bias=False): super().__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size if isinstance(kernel_size, tuple) else (kernel_size, kernel_size) self.stride = stride self.padding = padding if isinstance(padding, tuple) else (padding, padding) self.dilation = dilation if isinstance(dilation, tuple) else (dilation, dilation) self.use_bias = bias # Initialize weight like PyTorch (Kaiming uniform) self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels, in_channels, *self.kernel_size)) nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=0, mode='fan_in', nonlinearity='leaky_relu') # Transpose weight for our kernel: [IC, KH, KW, OC] self.weight_t = nn.Parameter(self.weight.permute(1, 2, 3, 0).contiguous()) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) nn.init.zeros_(self.bias) else: self.bias = None def forward(self, x): return conv2d_cuda.conv2d_forward( x, self.weight_t, self.bias, self.padding[0], self.padding[1], self.dilation[0], self.dilation[1] ) ``` Wait, there's an issue with `load_inline`. reasoning
The issue is that we shouldn't declare CUDA kernel prototypes in `cpp_sources` - those go in `cuda_sources`. The `cpp_sources` should only contain host functions. Let me simplify this approach and put everything in the CUDA source file or just keep the host code in cpp_sources without kernel declarations.

turn 4 · 1 tool call(s) · 30.3s · 14,938 chars (click to expand)
turn 4llm latency: 30.3s
reasoning
The `load_inline` function requires both `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. But since the CUDA file includes the C++ wrapper, maybe we can try including it as `cpp_sources` while being compiled with nvcc... actually, looking at the API more carefully: - `cpp_sources`: a string with C++ source code for the ATen bindings - `cuda_sources`: a string with CUDA source code The way I've been doing it with `cpp_sources` and then `cuda_sources` should work. But the error is that the C++ compiler sees `__global__` keywords. This means maybe `cpp_sources` is being compiled as a single file along with the C++ wrapper generated by PyTorch. Let me try a different approach: keep C++ in `cpp_sources` and CUDA in `cuda_sources`, but don't include `__global__` declarations in the C++ source - just extern declarations.

reasoning
I see the issue now. The load_inline function needs `cpp_sources` to have the function declarations, and all CUDA code needs to be in the `cuda_sources` file. But when using templates, they need to be visible in the cpp file too or everything needs to be in the .cu file. Let me put all the code in the CUDA source and add proper forward declarations in the cpp source. Actually, the better approach is to put everything in the CUDA source and keep the cpp source with just extern declarations. But the issue is that the cpp file needs to see the function declarations. I can use `extern "C"` or just regular C++ declarations. Let me try a simpler approach - put the CUDA kernel in one .cu file and the wrapper function in another .cu file so they're both compiled by nvcc.

Also, the input loading isn't perfectly coalesced (each thread loads its own input based on spatial position, so threads in a warp may have scattered accesses depending on how spatial positions are assigned). To maximize coalescing: - Assign threads consecutive spatial positions along W dimension. - Thread i computes spatial pixel (block_start + i). - For a given (ic, kh, kw), the input positions are: h_in = oh * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h, where oh depends on spatial position w_in = ow * stride_w - pad_w + kw * dil_w, where ow depends on spatial position For threads with consecutive spatial positions, the (ow) values are consecutive, so w_in values are consecutive (since they differ by stride_w). If stride_w = 1, then w_in values are consecutive. For h_in, since spatial positions are 1D linearized (n, oh, ow), consecutive spatial positions often have the same oh until ow wraps around. So for most iterations, threads in a warp will have the same h_in and consecutive w_in values (when they're in the same row of output). This means input loads are mostly coalesced! When ow wraps around to 0, consecutive threads might be at different oh, but since the warp processes 32 consecutive spatial pixels, at most one wrap-around can happen per warp, affecting at most a couple of threads. So input coalescing is very good. This kernel design might actually work well! Let me write it more carefully. For the kernel, I'll structure it like this: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int H, int W, int OC, int OH, int OW, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dil_h, int dil_w) { const int total_spatial = B * OH * OW; int sp = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (sp >= total_spatial) return; int tmp = sp; int ow = tmp % OW; tmp /= OW; int oh = tmp % OH; int n = tmp / OH; // Load bias for all 64 OCs float acc[64]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) { acc[oc] = bias ? bias[oc] : 0.0f; } const float* in_base = input + n * IC * H * W; const float* w_base = weight; // weight is OC-major: [OC][IC][KH][KW] for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { int h_in = oh * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { int w_in = ow * stride_w - pad_w + kw * dil_w; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue; float in_val = in_base[ic * H * W + h_in * W + w_in]; const float* w_ptr = w_base + ic * 5 * 9 * OC + kh * 9 * OC + kw * OC; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) { acc[oc] += in_val * w_ptr[oc]; } } } } // Write output float* out_base = output + n * OC * OH * OW + oh * OW + ow; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) { out_base[oc * OH * OW] = acc[oc]; } } ``` Wait, there are issues: 1. `acc[64]` uses 64 * 4 = 256 bytes of registers per thread. With 256 threads per block, that's a lot of register pressure. Max registers per SM on H100 = 64K * 4 bytes = 256KB. 256 threads * 255 max registers = about right. 64 floats plus other temp registers = maybe 80 registers per thread. That's within limits. 2. Register spilling might occur. If the compiler puts `acc` array to local memory, performance would tank. 3. Unrolling the oc loop 64 times generates a lot of instructions. This might cause instruction cache pressure. 4. Weight layout: I assumed [OC, IC, KH, KW]. If I use PyTorch's default layout [OC, IC, KH, KW], then `w_ptr[oc]` IS coalesced since consecutive oc values are adjacent in memory! 5. Each thread loading 64 consecutive floats from w_ptr - this IS coalesced within a warp if threads load consecutive elements. But each thread loads ALL 64 elements, not just its own. So thread 0 loads w_ptr[0..63], thread 1 loads w_ptr[0..63], etc. This is NOT coalesced across threads - each thread independently loads 64 elements. But since all threads in a warp load the SAME 64 addresses, the warp can broadcast/satisfy all threads from the same cache line. This is efficient! Wait, but if all threads in a warp access the SAME address, and that address is in L1, then it just needs 1 load from L1 (or a few cache lines) and broadcasts to all threads. This is very efficient! In fact, it's even better than coalesced loads of different addresses. Wait, but in my design, I said: ```cuda for (int oc = 0; oc < 64; ++oc) { acc[oc] += in_val * w_ptr[oc]; } ``` Each thread in the warp executes this same loop, accessing w_ptr[0], w_ptr[1], ..., w_ptr[63]. Since all 32 threads in a warp access w_ptr[0] simultaneously, the warp issues one load instruction for the whole warp, and the hardware broadcasts the loaded value to all threads. Wait no, does the hardware support this? Actually, for a memory instruction in a warp, if all threads access the SAME address, it's a "uniform" access and the hardware handles it with a single load. If threads access DIFFERENT addresses within the same cache line, it coalesces into one load. But if all threads access the same address, yes, it's handled very efficiently. HOWEVER, if we unroll this loop 64 times, each iteration generates a load instruction for the whole warp. Since all threads in the warp are loading the same element, the hardware broadcasts it. So 64 load instructions, each fetching 1 float (but actually a whole cache line, though subsequent elements are already in the same line or next line). Actually, consecutive `w_ptr[oc]` values occupy consecutive memory. If the cache line is 128 bytes (32 floats), then accessing w_ptr[0] loads 32 floats into cache, w_ptr[1] through w_ptr[31] are in cache, and w_ptr[32] loads another cache line. So 64 iterations would cause 2 cache misses and 62 cache hits. But actually, the FIRST thread to execute in the warp causes a load, and the L1 cache serves the line. Subsequent accesses by any thread in the warp hit L1. But if ALL threads in the warp execute simultaneously, the warp scheduler issues the load, and threads wait for it. Actually in modern GPUs, each warp executes the same instruction. If that instruction is a load, all threads in the warp participate. If they all access the same address, the hardware detects this and issues a single memory request (or a small number of requests for the cache line). But if they all access the SAME address, only ONE float is needed for the whole warp. Wait, but each thread needs the value for its own computation! Oh wait, I made a critical error. In my design, each thread computes ALL 64 OCs. So thread 0 computes acc[0], acc[1], ..., acc[63]. Thread 1 ALSO computes acc[0], acc[1], ..., acc[63]. This means threads in the same warp are doing REDUNDANT work! That's really bad. If each thread computes all 64 OCs, then for a warp of 32 threads computing the same spatial pixel, we'd have 32x redundancy. And the block size of 256 means 256x redundancy! Let me fix this. Better design: - EACH thread computes a SUBSET of OCs for 1 spatial pixel. - Thread i computes OC[i], OC[i+32], OC[i+64], ... or some partition. - For OC=64 and warp_size=32, each thread computes 2 OCs (e.g., oc0 and oc1). - This way, threads in a warp DON'T do redundant work. Corrected design: - Thread i computes oc0 = i and oc1 = i + 32 for 1 spatial pixel. - Total threads per block = 256, computing 256 spatial pixels. - For each (ic, kh, kw): - All threads load the SAME input value (broadcast via L1 or __shfl). - Thread i loads weight[oc0] and weight[oc1] (2 coalesced loads per warp, since consecutive i load consecutive oc). - Each thread does 2 FMAs. This is exactly what I analyzed earlier and it's correct! Kernel code: ```cuda // Thread i handles OC channels: i and i+32 const int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; // or threadIdx.x % 32... actually if blockDim.x > 32, each warp handles a different spatial pixel ``` Wait, in a block of 256 threads: - Thread 0..31: warp 0, spatial pixel 0, oc lanes 0..31 - Thread 32..63: warp 1, spatial pixel 1, oc lanes 0..31 - Thread 64..95: warp 2, spatial pixel 2, oc lanes 0..31 - ... Wait, if threadIdx.x maps directly to oc lane AND spatial pixel, then: threadIdx.x = spatial_local * 32 + lane, where spatial_local = threadIdx.x / 32. So each warp handles ONE spatial pixel, with 32 threads computing 64 OCs (2 per thread). Then for the grid: sp = blockIdx.x * (blockDim.x / 32) + (threadIdx.x / 32) So each thread block handles 8 spatial pixels (256 threads / 32 threads per warp = 8 warps = 8 spatial pixels). Let me rewrite: ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_kernel(...) { const int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; const int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; const int spatial_per_block = blockDim.x >> 5; // 8 for blockDim=256 const int sp = blockIdx.x * spatial_per_block + warp_id; if (sp >= total_spatial) return; int tmp = sp; int ow = tmp % OW; tmp /= OW; int oh = tmp % OH; int n = tmp / OH; const int oc0 = lane; const int oc1 = lane + 32; float acc0 = bias ? bias[oc0] : 0.0f; float acc1 = bias ? bias[oc1] : 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_in = oh * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_in = ow * stride_w - pad_w + kw * dil_w; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue; // This is done by all threads in the warp, but they all load the same address // The compiler might optimize this, or we can use __shfl float in_val = input[n * IC * H * W + ic * H * W + h_in * W + w_in]; const float* w_ptr = weight + (ic * KH * KW + kh * KW + kw) * OC; acc0 += in_val * w_ptr[oc0]; acc1 += in_val * w_ptr[oc1]; } } } float* out_base = output + n * OC * OH * OW + oh * OW + ow; out_base[oc0 * OH * OW] = acc0; out_base[oc1 * OH * OW] = acc1; } ``` Wait, the `in_val` load: all 32 threads in a warp load the same address. This is a "uniform load" and the hardware handles it well with a single memory request. Good. The w_ptr[oc0] and w_ptr[oc1] loads: thread i loads w_ptr[i] and w_ptr[i+32]. Threads in a warp load w_ptr[0], w_ptr[1], ..., w_ptr[31] - these are CONSECUTIVE, so it's a fully coalesced load of 32 floats = 128 bytes = 2-4 memory transactions (depending on cache line size). Similarly for w_ptr[i+32]. Actually, on an H100, a single warp can load up to 128 bytes in one transaction via the L1 cache. So loading 32 consecutive floats (128 bytes) takes 1 or 2 transactions. Very efficient. This design looks solid. Each warp does: - 1440 iterations of (ic, kh, kw) - Per iteration: 1 uniform input load, 2 coalesced weight loads (32+32 floats), 2*32=64 FMAs - Per iteration memory: input (4 bytes, effectively per warp) + weights (256 bytes, per warp) = ~260 bytes - Per iteration compute: 64 FMAs = 128 FLOPs - AI per warp: 128 / 260 = 0.49 FLOPs/byte... wait, that doesn't sound right. Wait, if each warp handles 1 spatial pixel and 64 OCs: - Per (ic, kh, kw): 64 output values * 1 FMA each = 64 FMAs = 128 FLOPs - Memory: 1 input float (4 bytes) + 64 weight floats (256 bytes) = 260 bytes - AI = 128 / 260 = 0.49 FLOPs/byte This is WAY below the ridge point! So a single warp is memory bound. BUT, with 8 warps per block, and assuming the weights stay in L1 cache: - Warp 0 loads the 64 weights, then warps 1-7 find them in L1. - Per block per iteration: 8 input loads (32 bytes? No, 8 warps * 1 float each, but they might be coalesced if spatial pixels are consecutive) + 64 weights (256 bytes) - Per block FMAs: 8 warps * 64 FMAs = 512 FMAs = 1024 FLOPs - If inputs are coalesced: memory = 8 * 4 = 32 bytes (if the 8 pixels have consecutive memory) + 256 bytes = 288 bytes - AI = 1024 / 288 = 3.56 FLOPs/byte Still below 20. So even with perfect L1 for weights across warps in a block, we'd be memory bound. But wait, 8 spatial pixels might not be enough for good L1 reuse. What if we use more? Let's try a block with 32 warps = 1024 threads. Then: - Per block: 32 spatial pixels, 64 OCs each - Per iteration: 32 input loads + 64 weight loads - FMAs: 32 * 64 = 2048 FMAs = 4096 FLOPs - Memory (assuming inputs coalesced): 32 * 4 = 128 bytes + 256 bytes = 384 bytes - AI = 4096 / 384 = 10.7 FLOPs/byte Still below 20. What about 64 warps = 2048 threads (max per block)? That's not possible since max threads per block is 1024. So even with a full block, we'd be memory bound at 10.7 FLOPs/byte. The peak throughput would be limited by memory bandwidth. At 3.35 TB/s and 10.7 FLOPs/byte, the max compute is 10.7 * 3.35 = ~35.8 TFLOPS, not 67 TFLOPS. But wait, there's more nuance. The weights might stay in L2 cache across blocks. Since total weights = 32 * 5 * 9 * 64 * 4 = 368KB per block, and if there are many blocks, the L2 cache (50MB on H100!) can hold ALL the weights. So once weights are in L2, subsequent accesses are L2 hits. But input activations are large (67MB) and each input pixel is used only once. So the main memory traffic is inputs + outputs. Total memory needed: - Input: B * IC * H * W * 4 = 8 * 32 * 512 * 512 * 4 = 268,435,456 bytes ≈ 256 MB - Output: B * OC * OH * OW * 4 = 8 * 64 * 512 * 512 * 4 = 536,870,912 bytes ≈ 512 MB - Weights: 368KB - Total: ~768 MB - Time at 3.35 TB/s: 0.23 ms But we have 12.4 GFLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS: 0.185 ms. So the operation is on the boundary between compute and memory bound. In practice, with caches, it might lean compute-bound. cuDNN is very good at scheduling this. For a custom kernel to beat cuDNN, we'd need to either: 1. Achieve very high compute utilization (hard without tensor cores) 2. Reduce memory traffic (e.g., using Winograd which cuDNN already does) 3. Have lower overhead for small problem sizes Actually, for B=8, IC=32, OC=64, this is a relatively small convolution in terms of compute compared to typical deep learning workloads. The overhead of cuDNN's algorithm selection and workspace setup might be visible. But for a single forward pass, it's probably negligible.
