But I need to be careful about weight layout. PyTorch conv2d weight layout: [OC, IC, KH, KW] I need to access weight[oc, ic, kh, kw] efficiently. If I loop over ic, kh, kw and for each access weight[oc0] and weight[oc1], this is NOT efficient with [OC, IC, KH, KW] layout. The stride between oc values is 1 (contiguous), so accessing weight[oc0] and weight[oc1] where oc0 and oc1 differ by 32 is NOT contiguous for the first access but IS for the pair. Wait: - weight[oc0] at address: base + ((ic * KH + kh) * KW + kw) * OC + oc0 - weight[oc1] at address: base + ((ic * KH + kh) * KW + kw) * OC + oc1 = previous + 32 If the weight layout is [OC, IC, KH, KW], then for a fixed (ic, kh, kw), the OC values are contiguous! So weight[oc0] and weight[oc0 + 32] are separated by 32 floats. If oc0 = lane (0..31) and oc1 = lane + 32, then: - w_ptr[oc0] for lane=0..31 = addresses base + 0, base + 1, ..., base + 31 → coalesced - w_ptr[oc1] for lane=0..31 = addresses base + 32, base + 33, ..., base + 63 → also coalesced Great! Both loads are coalesced. And for output layout [B, OC, OH, OW]: - out_base[oc0 * OH * OW] and out_base[oc1 * OH * OW] are separated by 32 * OH * OW floats - These are NOT close together, so writing 64 values per thread might have poor locality. Wait, but each thread only writes 2 output values (oc0 and oc1) for its spatial pixel. Thread 0 writes to oc0=0 and oc1=32. Thread 1 writes to oc0=1 and oc1=33. So within a warp: - First write: threads 0..31 write to output channels 0..31 respectively for spatial pixel p - Wait, if all threads in the warp are writing for the SAME spatial pixel p, then: - Thread i writes to output[n, oc0=i, oh, ow] and output[n, oc1=i+32, oh, ow] - For the first write: threads 0..31 write to channels 0..31 of the same spatial pixel - In memory layout [B, OC, H, W], channels 0..31 of the same pixel are at addresses: base + 0*OH*OW + pixel_offset, base + 1*OH*OW + pixel_offset, ... - These are strided by OH*OW = 262,144 floats apart! - This is extremely UNCOALESCED! Each thread writes to a completely different memory region. This is bad! My previous design assumed output pixels for different channels were close together, but in NCHW format, they're far apart. For output layout NCHW: output[n, oc, oh, ow] = n * OC * OH * OW + oc * OH * OW + oh * OW + ow So for the same (n, oh, ow) but different oc, the stride is OH*OW. If we want coalesced stores, we need threads in a warp to write to adjacent memory addresses. But for NCHW, adjacent memory for the same spatial position corresponds to adjacent (n, oh, ow) or adjacent oc? Actually: - If we fix n, oc and vary (oh, ow), consecutive spatial pixels might not be adjacent if the stride isn't 1. - Actually in NCHW, for fixed n, oc, the layout is row-major over (oh, ow). So output[n, oc, oh, ow] and output[n, oc, oh, ow+1] are adjacent. For our design where each warp handles one spatial pixel, and each thread handles 2 OCs, the writes for oc0 and oc1 go to very different memory locations (separated by 32 * OH * OW). This is not coalesced across threads in a warp, but each thread doing 2 separate writes is fine. However, the writes from 32 threads to 32 different channels of the same pixel are scattered across memory. Each write goes to a different 1MB-ish region. This is terrible for memory coalescing and cache utilization. To fix this, we need a different assignment: - Each warp should handle a range of OCs for a range of spatial pixels. - OR, we should transpose the output to [B, H, W, OC] format (NHWC). Using NHWC would be MUCH better for this kernel: - output[n, oh, ow, oc] = n * OH * OW * OC + oh * OW * OC + ow * OC + oc - For the same spatial pixel, oc values are adjacent! - If thread i writes to oc=i and oc=i+32, both are within a 64-float contiguous block. So the kernel should write in NHWC format! But wait, PyTorch uses NCHW by default for conv2d. If my kernel outputs NHWC, I'd need to permute it back to NCHW. PyTorch `permute` and `contiguous` calls might add overhead. But for large tensors, they're bandwidth-bound and relatively fast. The overhead might be worth it if the kernel itself is much faster. Actually, let me check: with 512MB output, permute + contiguous at 3.35 TB/s takes about 0.15 ms. If the conv itself takes >1 ms, the overhead of 0.15 ms is manageable. But if cuDNN gets 0.3 ms total, then adding permute would make us slower. Hmm, for the given problem size: - 12.4 GFLOPs at 67 TFLOPS = 0.185 ms theoretical minimum. - Memory bandwidth for output: 512MB at 3.35TB/s = 0.15 ms. - So the operation is roughly 0.2-0.3 ms. - Adding a permute of 0.15 ms would make it 0.35-0.45 ms. Unless cuDNN is also doing something similar internally, or unless our kernel is significantly faster, the permute overhead might not be worth it. Alternative: write output in NCHW format but with different thread assignment. If each warp computes a TILE of output channels (say 4 or 8 channels) for multiple spatial pixels, we can get coalescing. For example: - A block of 256 threads. - Threads arranged as 32x8: 32 spatial pixels across, 8 output channels down. - Each thread computes 1 spatial pixel and 1 output channel. - Total per block: 32 spatial pixels * 8 channels. Then for the 8 channels: - Thread (i, c) computes spatial pixel i, channel c - For output stores: all threads with the same c but different i write to adjacent spatial pixels in the same channel. This is coalesced! - For the 8 channels, we'd do 8 separate coalesced stores per spatial pixel. But then each thread only does 1440 FMAs, which is not enough to amortize the setup overhead. Alternatively: - Warp computes 32 spatial pixels, each for 1 output channel. - 64 warps to cover 64 channels. - Total threads = 64 * 32 = 2048 threads per block? No, max 1024. Hmm, this is getting complicated. Actually, for our specific problem, OC=64. If we have a block with 64 warps, that's 2048 threads > 1024 limit. With 32 warps (1024 threads), we could cover 32 channels. We'd need 2 blocks to cover 64 channels. But the bigger issue is still that each thread does only 1440 FMAs. With the warp launching overhead and memory loads, 1440 FMAs is about 2880 FLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS, that's 43 nanoseconds of compute per thread. But memory latency is hundreds of nanoseconds, so memory latency would dominate unless we have lots of warps to hide it. To hide memory latency, we need many warps in flight per SM. With 1024 threads per block and 32 warps, that's 32 warps per block. If we have multiple blocks per SM, we can have up to 64 warps per SM (H100 limit) or 2048 threads per SM. So with 32 warps per block, we need 2 blocks per SM to fill it. With 132 SMs, we need 264 blocks. Total threads launched = 264 * 1024 = 270,336, which is close to the total number of output values (134,217,728 / 64 channels = 2,097,152 spatial pixels; wait no, per channel there are 2,097,152 output pixels, and we're computing 32 channels per block, so 64 * 2,097,152 total output values...). Actually, if each block computes 32 channels × 32 spatial pixels = 1024 output values. Total output values = 134,217,728. So we need 134,217,728 / 1024 = 131,072 blocks. That's plenty. But back to output coalescing: if each warp of 32 threads computes 32 spatial pixels for 1 channel, then stores are coalesced (consecutive spatial pixels in the same channel are adjacent in memory for NCHW? No! In NCHW, consecutive spatial pixels in the same channel ARE adjacent: base + c * H * W + spatial_offset). Yes! In NCHW: output[n, c, h, w] = base + c * H * W + h * W + w So for fixed n, c, consecutive w values are adjacent, and h values are strided by W. In a warp computing 32 consecutive spatial pixels, if they have the same h and consecutive w, their stores ARE coalesced! Wait, but if they span multiple rows (e.g., w wraps from W-1 to 0, h increases by 1), then there's a small gap in memory. For W=512, 32 consecutive pixels fit within a row (since 32 < 512), so 32 consecutive pixels have the same h and w = w0..w0+31, which ARE contiguous in memory. Coalesced stores! So this design works for output: - One warp = 32 threads - Each thread computes 1 spatial pixel, 1 output channel - All threads in warp: same n, same c, h is the same or varies slightly, w is consecutive - Store to output[n, c, h, w] → coalesced! For computation, each warp computes 32 spatial pixels in 1 channel, doing 1440 FMAs per thread. But the weights are the SAME for all threads in the warp (since same channel). So weights are broadcast. For a block with 32 warps: - Each warp handles a different channel (or different set of channels) - Block handles 32 channels, each with 32 spatial pixels - Total output values per block: 32 × 32 = 1024 Wait, but 1024 threads per block computing 1024 output values (each thread 1 value). That's 1440 FMAs per thread. Is that enough compute to amortize the kernel launch? At 67 TFLOPS, 1440 FMAs per thread is negligible. But what matters is throughput: can we saturate the GPU with enough thread blocks? 131,072 blocks × 1024 threads = 134M threads. That's a lot. The GPU can run ~270K threads concurrently. So it would take 134M / 270K ≈ 500 waves of execution. That should be enough to hide latency. Actually, 1440 FMAs per thread at 67 TFLOPS → if fully utilizing the GPU, the kernel would run for about 12.4 GFLOPs / 67 TFLOPS = 0.185 ms. So this design could potentially work! Let me think about the actual kernel. ```cuda __global__ void conv2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int H, int W, int OC, int OH, int OW, int KH, int KW, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dil_h, int dil_w ) { const int total_spatial = B * OH * OW; // Grid layout: blocks cover tiles of spatial pixels and channels // Let each block handle a tile // Simpler approach: each thread handles ONE output value int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_outputs = B * OC * OH * OW; if (idx >= total_outputs) return; int tmp = idx; int ow = tmp % OW; tmp /= OW; int oh = tmp % OH; tmp /= OH; int c = tmp % OC; int n = tmp / OC; float acc = bias ? bias[c] : 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_in = oh * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_in = ow * stride_w - pad_w + kw * dil_w; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue; float in_val = input[((n * IC + ic) * H + h_in) * W + w_in]; float w_val = weight[((c * IC + ic) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } output[((n * OC + c) * OH + oh) * OW + ow] = acc; } ``` This is the simplest direct conv. But there are issues: 1. Each thread loads its own input and weights independently. 2. Weight loads for the same channel are uniform across warps computing the same channel - the hardware broadcasts them. 3. Input loads might be coalesced or not depending on how threads are mapped. In the default 1D thread assignment (blockDim.x = 256, blockIdx.x varying): - Thread i computes output[i], thread i+1 computes output[i+1] - These correspond to adjacent output values in memory. - For adjacent outputs: they share the same (n, c) and differ in spatial position by 1. - So adjacent threads access input at the same h_in but w_in differs by stride_w (which is 1). - This means input loads are COALESCED! Great! So this simple 1D kernel actually has: - Coalesced output stores - Coalesced input loads (mostly) - Uniform weight loads (all threads in warp load same weight for same c) The only issue is that each thread only does 1440 FMAs. But with 134M total threads (in terms of output values) and massive parallelism, this might still be decent. Wait, 1440 FMAs per thread is actually very little. A warp of 32 threads does 32*1440 = 46080 FMAs. At ~2 cycles per FMA on H100 FP32 units, that's ~92000 cycles. But the warp scheduler can handle many warps. The key question is whether we're compute or memory bound. For this kernel: - Per thread: 1440 iterations, each loading 1 input + 1 weight + 1 FMA - Uniform weight load: all 32 threads in a warp access the same weight address. The hardware fetches it once and broadcasts. So effectively 1 weight load per warp per iteration. - Input load: 32 threads access 32 consecutive addresses (assuming consecutive threads compute consecutive spatial pixels). That's 1-2 cache lines per warp per iteration. - Per warp per iteration: ~128-256 bytes of memory traffic, ~32 FMAs = 64 FLOPs - AI = 64 / 256 = 0.25 FLOPs/byte → memory bound. At 3.35 TB/s, the peak FLOPS we can achieve is 3.35 * 0.25 = 0.84 TFLOPS. That's terrible. So this kernel would run at ~0.84 TFLOPS, not 67 TFLOPS. Total time ≈ 12.4 GFLOPs / 0.84 TFLOPS = 14.8 ms. cuDNN probably runs in ~0.3 ms. So this would be 50x SLOWER. The problem is the memory bandwidth. We need to increase the FLOPs per byte by reusing data. REUSING WEIGHTS: If a block of threads all compute the SAME channel but DIFFERENT spatial pixels, then weights are fully shared (100% reuse). Input values differ. So for a block computing 1 channel × 256 spatial pixels: - Memory per iteration (ic, kh, kw): 256 input floats + 1 weight float - FMAs: 256 * 1 = 256 FMAs = 512 FLOPs - Memory: 256 * 4 + 1 * 4 = 1028 bytes - AI = 512 / 1028 = 0.5 FLOPs/byte Still bad. But what if a block computes MULTIPLE channels? Say 32 channels × 32 spatial pixels = 1024 threads. - For iteration (ic, kh, kw): - Inputs: 32 spatial pixels × 32 channels × 1 float? No, input is per ic, so it's shared across all output channels. - For a given (ic, kh, kw) and fixed spatial pixels, the input is the same regardless of output channel. - So for 32 spatial pixels: 32 input floats (each pixel needs its own input for this ic, kh, kw) - For 32 output channels: 32 weight floats (one per channel) - Total memory: 32 * 4 + 32 * 4 = 256 bytes - FMAs: 32 spatial × 32 channels = 1024 FMAs = 2048 FLOPs - AI = 2048 / 256 = 8 FLOPs/byte Better, but still below 20. What about 64 channels × 16 spatial pixels = 1024 threads? - Inputs: 16 floats - Weights: 64 floats - Memory: (16 + 64) * 4 = 320 bytes - FMAs: 16 * 64 = 1024 FMAs = 2048 FLOPs - AI = 2048 / 320 = 6.4 FLOPs/byte Worse. More spatial pixels with fewer channels gives better AI. What about 256 spatial pixels × 4 channels = 1024 threads? - Inputs: 256 floats - Weights: 4 floats - Memory: (256 + 4) * 4 = 1040 bytes - FMAs: 256 * 4 = 1024 FMAs = 2048 FLOPs - AI = 2048 / 1040 = 1.97 FLOPs/byte Even worse. So the best configuration from the options above was 32 spatial × 32 channels with AI=8. Still memory bound. To get AI > 20, we'd need to process more channels or spatial pixels within a block to amortize input loads. But the inputs differ for different spatial pixels, and the weights differ for different channels. Wait, what if we process the SAME spatial pixels for MORE channels? Say 128 channels × 8 spatial pixels = 1024 threads. But OC=64, so max channels is 64. If OC were larger... With OC=64 and 1024 threads: - If we use 8 spatial × 64 channels = 512 threads < 1024. Then we could do 16 spatial × 64 channels = 1024 threads. - Inputs: 16 floats - Weights: 64 floats - Memory: (16 + 64) * 4 = 320 bytes - FMAs: 16 * 64 = 1024 FMAs = 2048 FLOPs - AI = 2048 / 320 = 6.4 Not enough. To get better AI, we'd need to reuse either inputs or weights across multiple kernel positions. But in a direct conv, each (ic, kh, kw) position has DIFFERENT weights and might have DIFFERENT inputs. What about loading a tile of input into shared memory and reusing it across multiple output channels? For a block computing 16×16 spatial pixels across all 64 channels: - Load a 16×16 tile of input for one (ic, kh, kw) into shared memory. - Compute all 64 channels using the shared input. - Then move to next (ic, kh, kw). This way, inputs are loaded once and reused 64 times! For a block of 256 threads: - Compute 8×8 spatial pixels (64 pixels) across 64 channels = 4096 output values. - 256 threads, each handles 16 output values (e.g., 1 pixel × 16 channels, or 4 pixels × 4 channels). Let's say 256 threads, each handles 1 pixel for 16 channels. - Block = 16×16 output pixels? No, 256 threads handling 256 pixels total. - If we do 256 pixels × 1 channel = 256 pixels, need to loop over 64 channels. Not good for weight reuse. - If we do 8×8 pixels = 64 pixels × 16 channels = 1024 values per block. Total blocks = 134M / 1024 = 131K blocks. Each thread handles 4 output values. Actually let me think about shared memory approach more carefully. Shared memory input tile: For a block computing output pixels in range [oh_start, oh_start+tile_h) and [ow_start, ow_start+tile_w): The input region needed is larger due to the kernel size. For KH=5, KW=9, dil_h=2, dil_w=3: - The footprint for one output pixel covers h: [oh*stride-pad, oh*stride-pad+(KH-1)*dil], w similar. - For a tile of output pixels, the input region is roughly tile_h × stride_h + (KH-1)*dil_h and tile_w × stride_w + (KW-1)*dil_w. - For stride=1, dil_h=2, KH=5: input height = tile_h + 4*2 = tile_h + 8 - For dil_w=3, KW=9: input width = tile_w + 8*3 = tile_w + 24 If tile_h = tile_w = 8: - Input region = 16 × 32 - Shared memory for one IC plane = 16 * 32 * 4 = 2048 bytes - For the whole block computing one IC at a time, this is very manageable. Then for each IC: 1. Load input tile into shared memory (16×32 for tile 8×8 output) 2. Compute all output channels using shared input 3. Block has 256 threads, each can compute (8×8 pixels × 64 channels) / 256 = 16 output values per thread. - Say 1 pixel × 16 channels per thread. 4. Per IC: 256 input loads (but with reuse across channels), 64*64 = 4096 weight loads, 4096*5*9 = 184,320 FMAs? Wait... Actually, for each output pixel and channel, we need KH*KW = 45 FMAs. For 64 pixels × 64 channels: 64*64*45 = 184,320 FMAs. Wait, the input is shared, so we load it once per IC. For each IC: - Input loads: ~16*32 = 512 floats, but coalesced across threads, so maybe ~512*4 = 2048 bytes - For each (kh, kw): weights = 64 floats, and all pixels reuse them - Number of (kh, kw) iterations = 45 (but many might be outside the valid range due to padding) Actually, for the full conv: - Per IC, per output pixel, KH*KW = 45 multiply-adds. - With input sharing: load ~512 input pixels once. - Weight loads: 45 positions × 64 channels = 2880 floats. - FMAs: 64 pixels × 64 channels × 45 = 184,320 FMAs. - Memory: 512 input + 2880 weights = 3392 floats = 13,568 bytes. - FLOPs: 184,320 × 2 = 368,640 - AI = 368,640 / 13,568 = 27.2 FLOPs/byte NOW we're compute bound! 27.2 > 20! This is excellent. With proper shared memory tiling, we can be compute bound. And this is exactly what cuDNN does implicitly through its GEMM algorithms. But our custom kernel can be specialized for this exact kernel size, potentially avoiding some overhead. Let me design the shared memory kernel: BLOCK CONFIGURATION: - Block size: 256 threads - Output tile: 8×8 spatial pixels = 64 pixels - Channels per block: all 64 OC (or a subset if too much register pressure) Each thread handles: - 64 pixels / 256 threads = 0.25 pixels per thread... that doesn't work. Let's have each thread handle 1 output pixel for some channels. Better: - Output tile: 16×16 = 256 pixels - 256 threads, 1 pixel per thread - Each thread computes ALL 64 channels for its pixel = 64 outputs - Total outputs per block: 256 * 64 = 16,384 But each thread with 64 accumulators = 256 bytes of registers. Plus indexing registers. This might spill. Let's try a smaller tile: - Output tile: 8×8 = 64 pixels - 256 threads... that's 4 threads per pixel. - Each thread computes 16 channels for 1 pixel. 16 accumulators = 64 bytes. Very comfortable. - Total: 64 pixels × 64 channels = 4096 outputs per block. - Thread mapping: (threadIdx.x >> 4) gives pixel index (0..63), (threadIdx.x & 15) gives channel group (0..3, each group handles 16 channels). Wait, OC=64, so 4 groups of 16 channels = 64 channels total. Each thread in a group handles exactly 1 pixel and 16 channels. For the block: - 256 threads = 64 pixels × 4 channel groups - Thread i: pixel = i / 4, ch_group = i % 4, channels = [ch_group*16, ch_group*16 + 15] Shared memory for input tile: - Output tile: 8×8 - Input footprint: height = 8 + (5-1)*2 = 16, width = 8 + (9-1)*3 = 32 - One IC plane: 16 * 32 = 512 floats = 2048 bytes - With 227KB shared memory limit, we can potentially load multiple ICs, but let's start with 1 IC at a time. ALGORITHM per IC: 1. Load input tile of size 16×32 into shared memory - 512 floats, loaded by 256 threads → each thread loads 2 floats - Coalesced if we map threads properly 2. Compute FMAs for all 64 OC: - For each output pixel (8×8 = 64 pixels): - For each of the 45 kernel positions (kh, kw): - Read input from shared memory - Read weight from global memory - Fma into 64 accumulators per pixel - Each thread computes 16 channels, so 16 FMAs per kernel position per pixel. Wait, but if each thread handles only 1 pixel, it would do: - For its pixel: - For each (kh, kw): load input from shared memory, load 16 weights, do 16 FMAs - Per IC: 45 positions × 16 FMAs = 720 FMAs And a block does: 64 pixels × 64 channels × 45 = 184,320 FMAs total. For memory: - Input: 512 floats per IC (shared memory, loaded once per block per IC) - Weights: 45 positions × 64 channels × 32 ICs = 92,160 floats total for all ICs - Per IC: 45 × 64 = 2880 floats - Weight memory traffic per IC: 2880 × 4 = 11,520 bytes Total per block for all ICs: - Input traffic: 32 ICs × 512 floats = from global memory, but with shared memory it's 32 loads of 512 = 16,384 floats = 65,536 bytes - Weight traffic: 32 ICs × 2880 floats = 92,160 floats = 368,640 bytes - Total: 434,176 bytes - FMAs: 64 pixels × 64 channels × 32 ICs × 45 positions = 5,898,240 FMAs - FLOPs: 11,796,480 - AI = 11,796,480 / 434,176 = 27.17 FLOPs/byte Perfect, compute bound! Now, the question is whether the kernel can actually achieve high compute utilization with this design. Potential issues: 1. Bank conflicts in shared memory: if multiple threads read the same input value, that's broadcast (no conflict). If they read different values in the same bank, there are conflicts. For 16×32 shared memory array, bank size is 4 bytes, 32 banks. Column j is in bank j%32. If threads read consecutive w_in, they access consecutive banks → no conflicts. If they read the same w_in, broadcast → no conflicts. Should be mostly conflict-free. 2. Register pressure: each thread has 16 accumulators + a few temp registers = ~20 registers. 256 threads × 20 registers = 5120 registers per block. Max 64K per SM, so we can have many blocks per SM. Good. 3. Occupancy: 256 threads per block. For 2048 max per SM, that's 8 blocks per SM if registers allow. With 5120 registers per block, 64K / 5120 ≈ 12 blocks max, but limited to 8 by thread count. So 8 blocks × 256 threads = 2048 threads per SM. Full occupancy! 4. Instruction mix: many FMAs and some shared memory loads. Should be good. 5. Output coalescing: each thread writes 16 output values for 1 pixel. In NCHW format, output channel c at pixel (n, oh, ow) is at address base + c * OH * OW + oh * OW + ow. For 16 channels, the writes are strided by OH*OW = 262,144 floats apart. That's terrible for coalescing! Wait, this is the same problem as before! In NCHW, different channels of the same pixel are NOT adjacent. So to write output coalesced, we'd need to write in NHWC or have threads in a warp write to adjacent memory. With our thread mapping: 64 pixels × 4 channel groups = 256 threads. In a warp of 32 threads, what do they compute? - If we map threads as (pixel, ch_group): - Thread 0: pixel 0, ch_group 0 (channels 0-15) - Thread 1: pixel 0, ch_group 1 (channels 16-31) - Thread 2: pixel 0, ch_group 2 (channels 32-47) - Thread 3: pixel 0, ch_group 3 (channels 48-63) - Thread 4: pixel 1, ch_group 0 - ... In a warp of 32 threads: - Threads 0-3: pixel 0, channels 0-15, 16-31, 32-47, 48-63 - Threads 4-7: pixel 1, channels 0-15, 16-31, 32-47, 48-63 - ... When writing output, threads in the same warp write to completely different memory regions (different channels and/or different pixels). There's NO coalescing at all. This is bad. We need a thread mapping that ensures coalesced writes. For NCHW, coalesced writes happen when consecutive threads write to consecutive elements in the same channel. That means consecutive threads should compute the SAME channel and ADJACENT spatial pixels. So: - Warp 0: channel 0, pixels 0-31 - Warp 1: channel 0, pixels 32-63 - ... - Then channel 1, etc. But then each block would need to handle only a few channels to have enough threads. If a block has 256 threads and processes 1 channel, it handles 256 pixels per block. For 64 channels and 2,097,152 pixels: 2,097,152 / 256 = 8192 blocks per channel × 64 channels? No, total spatial pixels is 2,097,152. If each block handles 256 pixels in one channel, we need 2,097,152 / 256 = 8192 blocks for all spatial pixels in 1 channel. For 64 channels: 8192 * 64 = 524,288 blocks. Plenty. But then weight reuse across channels is poor unless we load shared memory and loop over channels. If a block handles 1 channel and 256 pixels: - Load input for 256 pixels into shared memory: ~ (tile_h+8) × (tile_w+24) floats, maybe ~32×48 = 1536 floats = 6KB. - For 1 channel, compute 256 pixels using the shared input. - Then move to the next channel, reload weights (different channel), but input can stay if we compute all channels for the same spatial region. Wait, if a block stays on the same spatial region and loops over channels: - Spatial region has, say, 256 pixels (e.g., 16×16). - Input footprint: ~24×40 = 960 floats (approx). - For each channel: - Load 64 weights (1 per kernel position... wait, per channel there are 5*9*32 weights if IC=32). Actually, for our problem: weight is [OC, IC, KH, KW]. For channel c, the weights for all input channels and kernel positions are 32*5*9 = 1440 floats. If we loop over OC in the block, we'd load 1440 floats per channel. Let's say block handles 16×16 = 256 spatial pixels. - Input shared memory: ~24×40 = 960 floats = 3840 bytes - For each of 64 channels: load 1440 weights = 5760 bytes - FMAs: 256 pixels × 45 positions × 32 IC = 368,640 FMAs per channel = 737,280 FLOPs - Total per block: 64 channels × 368,640 FMAs = 23,592,960 FMAs = 47,185,920 FLOPs - Total memory: 3840 input + 64 × 5760 weights = 3840 + 368,640 = 372,480 bytes - AI = 47,185,920 / 372,480 = 126.7 FLOPs/byte Oh wow, this is extremely compute bound! But there's a catch: - 1440 weights per channel × 64 channels = 92,160 weights total. - Loading all these weights = 368KB per block. - The GPU has L2 cache, so weights might stay cached across blocks, but each block loads all weights independently. - With 524,288 blocks and 92,160 weights per block, total weight traffic = 524,288 × 368KB. That's a lot, but with L2 cache, only the first block that accesses a weight brings it from DRAM, and subsequent blocks hit L2. So the total DRAM weight traffic is just 368KB total for the entire kernel! Ah! The weights are only ~368KB total. They fit easily in L2 cache. So once loaded into L2 by the first few blocks, all subsequent blocks get L2 hits. Therefore, the actual DRAM bandwidth for weights is negligible. So the real memory traffic is: - Input: each block loads its input tile once - Output: each block writes its output tile once - Weights: loaded once total into L2, then L2 hits For a block with 256 pixels: - Input tile: ~960 floats = 3840 bytes - Output tile: 256 pixels × 64 channels = 16,384 floats = 65,536 bytes - But output writes are in NCHW format and not easily coalesced if threads map by channel. Wait, if a block handles all 64 channels for 256 pixels, but with 256 threads, how do we map threads? Option A: 256 threads, each computes 1 pixel for all 64 channels. - Thread 0: pixel 0, channels 0..63 - Writes to output at addresses: base + c*spatial_size + pixel0 for c=0..63 - These writes are strided by spatial_size, so not coalesced. Option B: 256 threads, each computes 1 channel for 4 pixels. - Thread 0: channel 0, pixels 0,1,2,3 - Writes to output: base + 0*spatial_size + pixel0, pixel1, pixel2, pixel3 - Four stores, each separated by 1 float → partially coalesced! - Actually, in a warp of 32 threads: - 32 threads, each for a different channel, same 4 pixels. - Writing pixel0 for channels 0..31: 32 stores, each to base + c*spatial_size + pixel0 - These are strided by spatial_size → completely scattered, not coalesced at all! Option C: 256 threads, each computes 16 channels for 1 pixel (but 16 channels total). Wait, we need all 64 channels. How about: warp handles 1 channel for 32 consecutive pixels. - Warp 0: channel 0, pixels 0..31 - Thread i: pixel i, channel 0 - Writes to: base + 0*spatial_size + i - These ARE coalesced! All 32 writes go to consecutive addresses! - Warp 1: channel 0, pixels 32..63 - Also coalesced. - Then warp 2: channel 1, pixels 0..31 - Coalesced within the warp. For 64 channels and 256 pixels per block: - 64 channels × 8 warps (256 pixels / 32 pixels per warp) = 512 warps - But a block can have at most 1024 threads = 32 warps. - So we'd need 512 / 32 = 16 blocks to cover 64 channels × 256 pixels. Alternatively, a block has 32 warps = 1024 threads, covering 32 channels × 32 pixels = 1024 outputs. But we need 64 channels × 256 pixels = 16,384 outputs per block if we want to reuse the shared input tile. So a block with 32 warps can cover 32 channels × 32 pixels. To cover 64 channels, we need 2 such blocks processing the same spatial pixels but different channel ranges. Both blocks load the SAME input tile, but compute different output channels. This is fine since input is in shared memory. But wait, shared memory is per block, so both blocks would load the input tile independently. That's double the input bandwidth. Still manageable. For a block covering 32 channels × 32 pixels: - Shared memory input: ~32×48 ≈ 1536 floats = 6144 bytes (for 32 pixels, not 256) - Actually, for 32 output pixels arranged as, say, 8×4, the input tile is (8+8) × (4+24) = 16 × 28 = 448 floats = 1792 bytes - Threads: 1024 = 32 warps - Warp i: channel i, pixels 0..31 - Wait, a warp can only compute 1 channel for 32 pixels. So 32 warps = 32 channels for 32 pixels. Good. - Per channel: weights = 32 IC × 5 × 9 = 1440 floats = 5760 bytes - For 32 channels: 32 × 5760 = 184,320 bytes of weights (but from L2 cache for most) The output writes: each warp writes 32 consecutive pixels in 1 channel. Fully coalesced! And the compute: - 32 channels × 32 pixels × 32 IC × 45 positions - Wait, 32 IC? No, we're looping over IC too. - FMAs per block: 32 pixels × 32 channels × 32 IC × 45 positions = 1,474,560 FMAs = 2,949,120 FLOPs Hmm, but that's a lot of compute within one block. We need to loop over IC if we only have 1792 bytes of shared memory. Actually, we load the input for one IC into shared memory, compute all 32 channels using shared input, then move to the next IC. So per IC iteration: - Load input tile: 448 floats from global memory → shared memory - Compute: 32 pixels × 32 channels × 45 positions = 46,080 FMAs - Weight loads: 32 channels × 45 positions = 1440 floats - Total memory per IC: 448 input + 1440 weights = 1888 floats = 7552 bytes - FLOPs per IC: 46,080 × 2 = 92,160 - AI = 92,160 / 7552 = 12.2 FLOPs/byte For 32 ICs: 12.2 AI. Still slightly memory bound (ridge point = 20). Hmm. What if we load multiple ICs into shared memory? 448 floats per IC. For 4 ICs: 1792 floats = 7168 bytes. Then: - Compute per IC iteration (4 ICs): 32 pixels × 32 channels × 4 ICs × 45 positions = 184,320 FMAs - Memory: 1792 input + 5760 weights (for 4 ICs × 1440) = 7552 floats = 30,208 bytes - FLOPs: 184,320 × 2 = 368,640 - AI = 368,640 / 30,208 = 12.2 Same AI. The ratio remains the same. The issue is that weight memory scales with IC, and so does compute, keeping the ratio constant. To increase AI, we need to either: 1. Increase output pixels per block (more compute without more weights) 2. Decrease weight memory (not possible without changing the conv params) If we increase pixels: 128 pixels × 8 channels instead of 32×32. - 128 pixels, say 16×8, input tile = 24×32 = 768 floats per IC - 8 channels, weights = 8 × 45 × 32 IC = 11,520 floats total for all ICs - Compute: 128 * 8 * 32 * 45 = 1,474,560 FMAs - Memory: 32 ICs * 768 input + 11,520 weights = 24,576 + 11,520 = 36,096 floats = 144,384 bytes - Wait, weights for 8 channels across 32 ICs = 8 * 32 * 5 * 9 = 11,520 floats = 46,080 bytes... - FLOPs: 1,474,560 * 2 = 2,949,120 - AI = 2,949,120 / (24,576*4 + 11,520*4) = 2,949,120 / 144,384 = 20.4 FLOPs/byte Ooh! Just barely above 20. Compute bound! For a block: - 1024 threads - 128 pixels (e.g., 16×8), 8 channels = 1024 outputs - Threads: each handles 1 pixel for 1 channel (or 4 pixels for 2 channels, etc.) Wait, 128 pixels × 8 channels = 1024 outputs. With 1024 threads, each thread handles exactly 1 output. - Thread mapping: pixel = threadIdx.x / 8, channel = threadIdx.x % 8 - Warp of 32 threads: 4 pixels × 8 channels (if we map consecutively) Output writes: - Thread i writes to channel c = i % 8, pixel p = i / 8 - Consecutive threads in warp: i=0 pixel0 ch0, i=1 pixel0 ch1, i=2 pixel0 ch2, ... - Writes to: base + ch*spatial_size + pixel - These are strided by spatial_size, so NOT coalesced. To get coalesced writes, consecutive threads should write to adjacent memory. In NCHW, adjacent memory means same channel, adjacent spatial pixels. So thread mapping should be: - channel = threadIdx.x / 128, pixel = threadIdx.x % 128 - Then for a warp of 32 threads with channel=0 and pixels 0..31: - Writes to base + 0*spatial_size + 0, base + 0*spatial_size + 1, etc. - These ARE coalesced! But we only have 8 channels per block. With 1024 threads: - channel = threadIdx.x / 128, where channel ranges 0..7 - pixel = threadIdx.x % 128, where pixel ranges 0..127 - Thread 0: ch0, pix0 - Thread 127: ch0, pix127 - Thread 128: ch1, pix0 - etc. For a warp of 32 threads: - Threads 0..31: ch0, pix0..31 → coalesced writes - Threads 32..63: ch0, pix32..63? No, warp size is 32. - Warp 0: threads 0..31 = ch0, pix0..31 → coalesced! - Warp 1: threads 32..63 = ch0, pix32..63 → coalesced! (but wait, the warp doesn't have all 32 threads contiguous in channel...) Actually, in this mapping: - Thread i = channel * 128 + pixel - For thread 0..31: channel = 0, pixel = 0..31 - For thread 32..63: channel = 0, pixel = 32..63 - For thread 64..95: channel = 0, pixel = 64..95 - ... - Thread 128..159: channel = 1, pixel = 0..31 - etc. Wait, this means a block covers 8 channels × 128 pixels = 1024 outputs. But the warps within the block process entire channel slices of 128 pixels. Each warp handles 32 pixels in one channel. So: - Warp 0: channel 0, pixels 0..31 - Warp 1: channel 0, pixels 32..63 - Warp 2: channel 0, pixels 64..95 - Warp 3: channel 0, pixels 96..127 - Warp 4: channel 1, pixels 0..31 - etc. For coalesced output writes AND weight sharing, this is good because all threads in a warp are in the SAME channel. They share weights (same channel, different spatial pixels). But input sharing across channels is reduced because each warp computes a different channel. However, different channels for the SAME spatial pixels use the SAME input. So if we process all 8 channels for the same 128 pixels, the input is reused across all 8 channels. With 32 warps per block: - 8 channels × 4 warps per channel (128 pixels / 32 = 4 warps per channel) - Warp layout: channel = warp_id / 4, pixel_group = warp_id % 4 - All warps in the same channel compute the same weights, different pixel groups. For shared memory input: - All 32 warps read from the SAME shared memory tile. - Input tile size for 128 pixels: depends on pixel arrangement. - If pixels are a 16×8 tile, input tile is ~24×32 = 768 floats per IC. - Shared memory: 768 floats × 4 bytes = 3072 bytes per IC. Algorithm: ```cuda // Block: 1024 threads (32 warps) // Output tile: 8 channels × 128 pixels // Pixels arranged as 16×8 __shared__ float in_shared[24][32]; // For current IC for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { // Load input tile for this IC into shared memory // 24*32 = 768 floats, loaded by 1024 threads (each loads 0 or 1) // Actually we need ~16*32 but with footprint... __syncthreads(); // Each warp computes 32 pixels in 1 channel int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int channel = warp_id >> 2; // 0..7 int pixel_group = warp_id & 3; // 0..3 int pixel_local = pixel_group * 32 + lane; // 0..127 // Convert pixel_local to oh, ow int oh = pixel_local / 8; // 0..15 (if tile is 16×8) int ow = pixel_local % 8; // 0..7 float acc = bias ? bias[block_channel_start + channel] : 0.0f; for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { // Compute input position int h_in = oh * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h + tile_oh_start; int w_in = ow * stride_w - pad_w + kw * dil_w + tile_ow_start; // Read from shared memory int sh_h = h_in - tile_h_start; int sh_w = w_in - tile_w_start; float in_val = in_shared[sh_h][sh_w]; // Read weight float w_val = weight[((block_channel_start + channel) * IC + ic) * KH * KW + kh * KW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } // Write output int global_oh = tile_oh_start + oh; int global_ow = tile_ow_start + ow; int out_idx = ((n * OC + block_channel_start + channel) * OH + global_oh) * OW + global_ow; output[out_idx] = acc; __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, there are issues: 1. Each thread computes only 1 output value (1 pixel, 1 channel). So acc is a single float. 2. But we need to loop over all IC values, accumulating into acc. 3. And we need to be careful about __syncthreads() since some threads might not participate if pixel is out of bounds. Actually, with 16×8 pixels, we have exactly 128 pixels. If output OH=OW=512, a 16×8 tile fits exactly for 512/16=32 tiles in H and 512/8=64 tiles in W. So no out-of-bounds issues for interior tiles. Edge tiles might have fewer pixels, but the problem says padding keeps spatial size the same, so OH=H=512, OW=W=512. Also, threads in a warp all compute valid pixels (since 128 pixels = 4 warps of 32, and each warp handles 32 pixels in one group). No divergence. But there is a major issue: the weight access pattern. `weight[((c * IC + ic) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]` For channel c, all threads in the block access weights for the same channel c. Within a warp, they access the SAME weight. The hardware broadcasts this. For channel c+1, a different warp accesses a different weight. Since these are sequential in the weight layout (channels are adjacent), they might be in the same cache line. Good. Now, can we fit 8 channels in a block with 32 warps? - 32 warps = 1024 threads - 8 channels × 4 warps per channel = 32 warps. Yes. And for output writes: - Warp in channel c, pixel group g: threads write to pixels g*32..g*32+31 in channel c. - These ARE coalesced! Great. Input shared memory: 768 floats = 3072 bytes. This is per IC. We loop over IC, reloading shared memory each time. Thread mapping to load shared memory: - 768 floats, 1024 threads. 256 threads don't load anything? Actually we can have each thread load at most 1 float, with 256 threads idle. Or we can have 768 threads each load 1 float. - For coalesced global loads, assign thread i to load input[h_start + i / 32][w_start + i % 32], but the global input footprint might not be a simple rectangle. More precisely, if the output tile is [oh_start, oh_start+16) × [ow_start, ow_start+8), the input region is: h: [oh_start - pad_h, oh_start - pad_h + (5-1)*2 + 16) = [oh_start - 2, oh_start + 24) Wait, stride=1, pad=(2,4), dil=(2,3): oh * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h for kh in [0,4] and oh in [oh_start, oh_start+16) Minimum h_in = oh_start*1 - 2 + 0*2 = oh_start - 2 Maximum h_in = (oh_start+15)*1 - 2 + 4*2 = oh_start + 15 - 2 + 8 = oh_start + 21 So h range: 24 values (from oh_start-2 to oh_start+21 inclusive) w range: Minimum w_in = ow_start*1 - 4 + 0*3 = ow_start - 4 Maximum w_in = (ow_start+7)*1 - 4 + 8*3 = ow_start + 7 - 4 + 24 = ow_start + 27 So w range: 32 values (from ow_start-4 to ow_start+27 inclusive) Total input region: 24 × 32 = 768 floats. Matches what I computed! Loading 768 floats into shared memory: ```cuda for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 24 * 32; i += blockDim.x) { int sh_h = i / 32; int sh_w = i % 32; int h = oh_start - 2 + sh_h; int w = ow_start - 4 + sh_w; if (h >= 0 && h < H && w >= 0 && w < W) { in_shared[i] = input[((n * IC + ic) * H + h) * W + w]; } else { in_shared[i] = 0.0f; } } ``` Wait, I should use a 2D shared memory array: `__shared__ float in_shared[24][32];` And thread mapping: ```cuda for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 24 * 32; i += blockDim.x) { int sh_h = i / 32; int sh_w = i % 32; ... in_shared[sh_h][sh_w] = ...; } ``` This is fully coalesced because consecutive threads load consecutive w values, which are consecutive in global memory for fixed h. For the compute part: ```cuda // After __syncthreads() int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int channel_in_block = warp_id >> 2; // 0..7 int pixel_in_warp = lane; // 0..31 int pixel_in_block = (warp_id & 3) * 32 + pixel_in_warp; // 0..127 int oh = pixel_in_block / 8; int ow = pixel_in_block % 8; int c = block_channel_start + channel_in_block; float acc = bias ? bias[c] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { int sh_h = oh + kh * 2; // Because dil_h=2, and we've offset shared memory appropriately // Wait, shared memory h index needs to account for starting offset. // We loaded from oh_start-2, so h_in = oh_start + oh (where oh is 0..15) // For output at oh, the input for kernel position kh is at: // h_in = (oh_start + oh) - 2 + kh * 2 = oh_start + oh + 2*kh - 2 // In shared memory, this is at index: h_in - (oh_start - 2) = oh + 2*kh // So sh_h = oh + kh * dil_h #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { int sh_w = ow + kw * 3; // Similarly, sh_w = ow + kw * dil_w float in_val = in_shared[sh_h][sh_w]; float w_val = weight[((c * IC + ic) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } ``` Wait, the indexing assumes `in_shared` is loaded with the proper offset so that sh_h = oh + kh*2 accesses the right element. Let's verify: For output pixel at (oh_start + oh, ow_start + ow): h_in = (oh_start + oh) * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h = oh_start + oh - 2 + kh * 2 Shared memory was loaded starting at h_global = oh_start - 2. So the shared memory index for h_in is: sh_h = h_in - (oh_start - 2) = oh_start + oh - 2 + kh*2 - oh_start + 2 = oh + kh*2 Similarly for w: sh_w = ow + kw * 3 This is correct! And for w_in: w_in = ow_start + ow - 4 + kw * 3 Shared memory loaded from w_global = ow_start - 4. sh_w = w_in - (ow_start - 4) = ow + kw * 3. Correct. So the shared memory access pattern is very simple: `in_shared[oh + kh * 2][ow + kw * 3]` And for `oh` in [0, 15] and `kh` in [0, 4], `sh_h` ranges 0..23. For `ow` in [0, 7] and `kw` in [0, 8], `sh_w` ranges 0..31. Which matches our shared memory dimensions! No bounds checking needed inside the loop. The only bounds check needed is during the load into shared memory: ```cuda int h_global = oh_start - 2 + sh_h; int w_global = ow_start - 4 + sh_w; if (h_global >= 0 && h_global < H && w_global >= 0 && w_global < W) { in_shared[sh_h][sh_w] = input[...]; } else { in_shared[sh_h][sh_w] = 0.0f; } ``` Since oh_start varies from 0 to 511 in steps of 16: - oh_start - 2 ranges from -2 to 509 - For oh_start = 0: h_global ranges -2 to 21. Negative values need zero padding. - For oh_start = 496: h_global ranges 494 to 517. Values >= 512 need zero padding. Similarly for w. Now, the issue with zero padding: when the input tile extends beyond the image boundaries, we load 0.0f into shared memory. This handles padding automatically in the kernel! Brilliant. So the kernel doesn't need to worry about boundary conditions during the main compute loop at all. It only needs to handle them during shared memory preload. This is a major advantage. The inner loop can be fully unrolled with no branches! Let me count again: - oh in [0, 15], kh in [0, 4] → sh_h in [0, 23] - ow in [0, 7], kw in [0, 8] → sh_w in [0, 31] - Inner loop: 5*9 = 45 iterations - Each iteration: 1 shared memory load, 1 global weight load, 1 FMA - Thread handles 1 output value - FMAs per thread: 45 Wait, if each thread computes 1 output value for 1 channel and 1 pixel, and there are 45 kernel positions, then each thread does 45 FMAs. That's very little! But wait, we also loop over IC: ```cuda for (int ic = 0; ic < 32; ++ic) { // load shared memory for this ic // synchronize // each thread does 45 FMAs } ``` Total FMAs per thread: 32 * 45 = 1440. That's the same as the direct approach! So the shared memory doesn't increase the arithmetic per thread - it just restructures the memory access to be more efficient. However, because the inner loop has no branches (no boundary checks), and the shared memory loads have good locality, the performance should be much better than the scattered global memory approach. But with only 1440 FMAs per thread, the problem is still about whether we can hide latency. The shared memory approach saves a lot of bandwidth but doesn't fundamentally change the arithmetic intensity per thread. Actually wait - the shared memory approach reuses the loaded input tile across multiple channels! In the direct approach, each thread loaded its own input independently, meaning the same input pixel was loaded multiple times by threads computing different channels for the same pixel. With shared memory, the input pixel is loaded ONCE total and shared by all channels in the block. This is where the memory bandwidth savings come from. But in terms of FLOPs per thread, it's still 1440. Actually, in the shared memory approach, each thread only computes 1 output value, so 1440 FMAs. But there are 1024 threads per block, and many blocks. Total FLOPs per block: 1024 × 1440 = 1,474,560 FMAs. This is the same total FLOPs regardless of approach. So the question is: can we reorganize to have each thread do MORE FMAs? Option: each thread computes MULTIPLE output values. - For example, each thread could compute 1 pixel for 4 channels, or 4 pixels for 1 channel. - If 1 pixel × 4 channels: 4 × 1440 = 5760 FMAs per thread. Much better for amortizing overhead. With 1024 threads per block and each doing 1 pixel × 4 channels: - Block covers 256 pixels × 4 channels = 1024 outputs - Or better: 128 pixels × 8 channels = 1024 outputs. Let me try: 128 pixels × 8 channels per block, 1024 threads, each thread 1 pixel × 1 channel. That's what I had. If each thread does 4 channels for 1 pixel: 256 threads per block, 256 pixels, 4 channels each = 1024 outputs. Then total blocks would be higher, but each thread does 5760 FMAs. Better for thread-level efficiency. But then the output writes: - Thread i: pixel = i / 4? Wait, this gets complicated. Actually, another consideration: if we use more channels per thread, we need more registers for accumulators (4 instead of 1). That's still fine. And weight loads increase by 4, but they're sequential and likely cached. For the output writing to be coalesced, consecutive threads should write to adjacent memory. In NCHW, adjacent memory is same channel, adjacent pixel. So if we have a warp of 32 threads: - All computing the same 4 channels for different pixels - Or all computing the same pixel for adjacent channels The first option gives coalesced output writes (if threads handle adjacent pixels in the same channel). The second gives coalesced weight loads but scattered output writes. Actually, can we just use NHWC output format? If we write output as [B, H, W, OC], then: - output[n, h, w, c] = base + (n * H * W + h * W + w) * OC + c - For the same pixel, adjacent channels are adjacent in memory! - If a thread computes 1 pixel for 4 adjacent channels, and the next thread computes the same pixel for channels 4..7, then output writes are: - Thread 0: base + pixel_offset * OC + 0, base + pixel_offset * OC + 1, base + pixel_offset * OC + 2, base + pixel_offset * OC + 3 - Thread 1: base + pixel_offset * OC + 4, ... - These are NOT coalesced because threads in a warp write to the same pixel but different channels (offset by 1, 2, 3, etc.). Wait, for coalesced writes, the address difference between thread i and thread i+1 should be 1 float. So: - Thread i: base + pixel_offset_i * OC + c_i - For this to be contiguous: pixel_offset_i * OC + c_i = i - c_i = i % OC, pixel_offset_i = i / OC So we need threads to naturally map to linear output memory. Option: use 1D thread mapping where thread i computes output[i] directly. - output[i] = n * OC * H * W + c * H * W + spatial - If threads i and i+1 compute adjacent output elements, their spatial positions differ by 1, or c differs by 1. - If they differ in spatial by 1 (same c), then input positions also differ by 1, which is coalesced for input. - But weights differ because c differs... wait, if they have the same c, then weights are the same. So a natural 1D assignment where thread i computes output channel c = i / total_spatial, spatial = i % total_spatial: - Thread i and i+1: same channel, adjacent spatial → same weights, adjacent input, adjacent output. - Coalesced for input, output, and uniform for weights! - But then each block covers many channels (1024 threads / 32768 spatial = very few channels per block). Actually, total spatial = 2,097,152. If thread i computes output[i], then for a block of 256 threads, it covers 256 consecutive output elements. If these are all in the same channel and adjacent spatial positions, then: - Channel c, spatial positions p..p+255 - Threads load input for positions p..p+255 (coalesced) - Threads load the SAME weights (uniform/broadcast) - Threads write output for positions p..p+255 (coalesced) - Compute per thread: 1440 FMAs This is simple and effective! The only issue is that each block covers only 256 outputs in a single channel. But there are many blocks. And since weights are the same for the whole block, they can be prefetched or stay in L1/L2. Actually, this is a very nice design for NCHW! Let me formalize it: ```cuda // Total outputs = B * OC * OH * OW // Thread i computes output[i] // Grid covers all outputs // For a warp of 32 threads: // All compute the same channel c // Spatial positions are p, p+1, ..., p+31 // Per thread: // channel c, spatial s // Spatial s corresponds to (n, oh, ow) // Algorithm: for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { // Load input for this (ic, kh, kw) across all threads // Coalesced because consecutive spatial positions float in_val = input[...]; // Load weight float w_val = weight[...]; // FMA acc += in_val * w_val; } } } ``` For this design, consecutive threads in a warp have consecutive ow (or mostly consecutive, wrapping around oh every 512). So input loads are coalesced (consecutive addresses in the W dimension). Output writes are to consecutive addresses in the same channel → coalesced. Weight loads are the same address for all threads in a warp → uniform load, very efficient. This design seems excellent! And it's MUCH simpler than shared memory tiling. The key is that it naturally matches the NCHW memory layout. The arithmetic intensity: - Per warp per iteration (ic, kh, kw): - Input: 32 floats = 128 bytes (coalesced) - Weight: 1 float = 4 bytes (broadcast, effectively 4 bytes) - FMAs: 32 - Memory: ~132 bytes - AI = 64 / 132 = 0.48 FLOPs/byte Wait, that's terrible! Per warp it's memory bound. But across a block of 256 threads: - The block handles 256 spatial pixels in 1 channel. - For a given (ic, kh, kw): - Input: 256 floats = 1024 bytes - Weight: 1 float = 4 bytes (same for all 256 threads) - FMAs: 256 - Memory: 1028 bytes - AI = 512 / 1028 = 0.5 FLOPs/byte Still terrible. So while the kernel is simple, it's memory bound. To improve AI, we'd need to compute multiple channels per block. If a block handles 4 channels and 64 pixels each: - 4 channels × 64 pixels = 256 outputs = 256 threads - For (ic, kh, kw): - Input: 64 floats (broadcast across 4 channels) = 256 bytes - Weights: 4 floats = 16 bytes - FMAs: 64 × 4 = 256 - AI = 512 / 272 = 1.88 Still low. To reach AI > 20, we need to significantly increase FMAs per byte. Using shared memory is the way. But even with shared memory, for a block with 256 threads computing 256 pixels × 1 channel = 256 outputs: - Per IC iteration: load 256 pixels input shared once - For 1 channel, weights = 45 floats - FMAs = 256 × 45 = 11,520 - Memory = 256 input + 45 weights = 301 floats = 1204 bytes - AI = 23,040 / 1204 = 19.1 Close to 20! And if we compute 2 channels per block (128 pixels each): - Input: 128 floats shared - Weights: 2 channels × 45 = 90 floats - FMAs: 128 × 2 × 45 = 11,520 - Memory: 128 + 90 = 218 floats = 872 bytes - AI = 23,040 / 872 = 26.4 Better! So shared memory with ~2 channels per block and ~128 pixels gives AI > 20. And for a block with 256 threads: - 128 pixels × 2 channels = 256 outputs. Each thread computes exactly 1 output. - Threads in a warp: if we map threads to (pixel, channel) with channel varying slowest: - Warp: pixels 0..31, channel 0 for threads 0..31, then channel 1 for threads 32..63 - Wait, a warp is 32 threads. If threads 0..31 are all channel 0 and consecutive pixels, output writes are coalesced. - Threads 32..63 are channel 1, consecutive pixels. Also coalesced. - This means each block has 8 warps (256 threads), where: - Warp 0-1: channel 0, pixels 0-63 - Warp 2-3: channel 1, pixels 0-63 - Total: 2 channels × 128 pixels. Output writes: - Warp 0: channel 0, pixels 0..31 → coalesced - Warp 1: channel 0, pixels 32..63 → coalesced - Warp 2: channel 1, pixels 0..31 → coalesced - etc. Excellent! Input shared memory for 128 pixels: - If pixels are arranged as 16×8 or 8×16: - For 8×16 output tile: input tile footprint - h: 8 + 8 = 16 - w: 16 + 24 = 40 - Total: 640 floats = 2560 bytes - For 16×8 output tile: input tile footprint - h: 16 + 8 = 24 - w: 8 + 24 = 32 - Total: 768 floats = 3072 bytes Let's use 8×16 = 128 pixels, input tile 16×40 = 640 floats. Loading shared memory: ```cuda __shared__ float in_smem[16][40]; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 16 * 40; i += blockDim.x) { int sh_h = i / 40; int sh_w = i % 40; int h = oh_start - 2 + sh_h; int w = ow_start - 4 + sh_w; if (h >= 0 && h < H && w >= 0 && w < W) { in_smem[sh_h][sh_w] = input[((n * IC + ic) * H + h) * W + w]; } else { in_smem[sh_h][sh_w] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); ``` Compute: ```cuda int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; int channel = warp_id >> 1; // 0 or 1 int pixel_group = warp_id & 1; // 0 or 1 int pixel = pixel_group * 32 + lane; // 0..63 in the tile int oh = pixel / 16; int ow = pixel % 16; int c = block_channel_start + channel; float acc = bias ? bias[c] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { int sh_h = oh + kh * 2; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { int sh_w = ow + kw * 3; float in_val = in_smem[sh_h][sh_w]; float w_val = weight[((c * IC + ic) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } ``` Wait, sh_w = ow + kw * 3. For ow in [0, 15] and kw in [0, 8]: - Max sh_w = 15 + 24 = 39 - Min sh_w = 0 + 0 = 0 And shared memory is [16][40]. For sh_h: - oh in [0, 7], kh in [0, 4] - Max sh_h = 7 + 8 = 15 - Min sh_h = 0 + 0 = 0 Matches perfectly! And the pixel mapping: - pixel = pixel_group * 32 + lane - For pixel_group=0, lane=0..31: pixels 0..31 - oh = 0..1, ow = 0..15 - For pixel_group=1, lane=0..31: pixels 32..63 - oh = 2..3, ow = 0..15 So the 8×16 tile covers oh=0..7, ow=0..15. Pixels are row-major. Output writes: ```cuda int global_oh = oh_start + oh; int global_ow = ow_start + ow; output[((n * OC + c) * OH + global_oh) * OW + global_ow] = acc; ``` For a warp in channel c, pixel_group g: - Threads write to: ((n*OC + c)*OH + oh_start + g*2 + lane/16) * OW + ow_start + lane%16 - If lane increases by 1, ow increases by 1 (since lane/16 is 0 for lanes 0..15, and 1 for lanes 16..31) - But within a warp of 32 threads, lane/16 is 0 or 1, so threads 0..15 write to oh=g*2, and threads 16..31 write to oh=g*2+1 - This means the warp writes to TWO rows, not one! That's not strictly coalesced across the whole warp. Hmm, but actually: - Threads 0..15: oh = g*2, ow = 0..15. These write to consecutive addresses within row g*2. - Threads 16..31: oh = g*2+1, ow = 0..15. These write to consecutive addresses within row g*2+1. - The addresses in row g*2 and g*2+1 are separated by OW = 512 floats = 2048 bytes. On NVIDIA GPUs, a warp can issue memory transactions for non-consecutive addresses as long as they're within the same cache line segments. For addresses separated by 2048 bytes, the warp might issue two separate memory transactions (one for threads 0..15, one for threads 16..31). Within each group of 16 threads, the addresses are fully consecutive, so each group is perfectly coalesced. With a cache line of 128 bytes, 16 consecutive floats = 64 bytes = half a cache line. So each group of 16 threads issues one transaction per 16 floats? Actually, 16 floats = 64 bytes, which might fit in one or two cache line transactions depending on alignment. Either way, it's very efficient. So while not perfectly coalesced across the full warp, it's still quite efficient. To make it even better, we could redesign the thread mapping so that each warp handles exactly 32 consecutive pixels in a single row: - 8×16 tile has 8 rows of 16 pixels each. - A warp handles 32 pixels, which spans 2 full rows (16 + 16). - So warp g handles rows 2g and 2g+1? That's what I have. - Actually, we could make the tile 4×32 = 128 pixels. - Then a warp of 32 threads handles exactly 1 row of 32 pixels. - Output writes would be perfectly coalesced! Let me try tile 4×32 = 128 pixels. Output tile: oh in [0, 4), ow in [0, 32) Wait, 4 rows × 32 cols = 128 pixels. Yes. Input footprint: - h range: oh_start - 2 to oh_start - 2 + 3 + 8 = oh_start + 9. Total 12 values. Wait, for oh in [0, 3], max h_in = oh_start + 3 - 2 + 4*2 = oh_start + 3 - 2 + 8 = oh_start + 9. So h range: oh_start - 2 to oh_start + 9. Total 12 values. - w range: ow_start - 4 to ow_start - 4 + 31 + 24 = ow_start + 51. Total 56 values. Wait, for ow in [0, 31], max w_in = ow_start + 31 - 4 + 24 = ow_start + 51. So w range: ow_start - 4 to ow_start + 51. Total 56 values. - Shared memory: 12 × 56 = 672 floats = 2688 bytes. Thread mapping: - Warp 0: channel 0, row 0, cols 0..31 - Warp 1: channel 0, row 1, cols 0..31 - Warp 2: channel 0, row 2, cols 0..31 - Warp 3: channel 0, row 3, cols 0..31 - Warp 4: channel 1, row 0, cols 0..31 - etc. For 2 channels: 8 warps = 256 threads. Output writes for a warp: - All threads in warp write to the same row, consecutive columns. - Fully coalesced! One memory transaction for the whole warp (128 bytes for 32 floats). This is perfect! Shared memory access during compute: - Thread lane computes oh = row, ow = lane - sh_h = oh + kh * 2 - sh_w = ow + kw * 3 - For oh in [0, 3] and kh in [0, 4]: sh_h in [0, 11] - For ow in [0, 31] and kw in [0, 8]: sh_w in [0, 55] - Shared memory dimensions [12][56]. Loading shared memory: ```cuda for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 12 * 56; i += blockDim.x) { int sh_h = i / 56; int sh_w = i % 56; int h = oh_start - 2 + sh_h; int w = ow_start - 4 + sh_w; if (h >= 0 && h < H && w >= 0 && w < W) { in_smem[sh_h][sh_w] = input[((n * IC + ic) * H + h) * W + w]; } else { in_smem[sh_h][sh_w] = 0.0f; } } ``` The total memory for this block configuration: - 128 pixels × 2 channels × 32 IC × 45 positions = 368,640 FMAs - Input loads (from DRAM to shared): 32 ICs × 672 floats = 21,504 floats = 86,016 bytes - Weight loads: 2 channels × 32 ICs × 45 = 2,880 floats = 11,520 bytes - Output writes: 128 pixels × 2 channels = 256 floats = 1,024 bytes - Total DRAM traffic: ~98,560 bytes - FLOPs: 737,280 - AI = 737,280 / 98,560 = 7.48... wait, that's way lower! I made an error! The total FLOPs for 128 pixels × 2 channels × 32 ICs × 45 positions is 128*2*32*45 = 368,640 FMAs = 737,280 FLOPs. But the weight loads of 2,880 floats assumes all weight accesses come from DRAM, which they don't! Weights are cached in L2. Once loaded by the first block, subsequent blocks get L2 hits. So the true DRAM traffic is just: - Input: 86,016 bytes (each block loads its own input) - Output: 1,024 bytes - Weights: Only the first time each weight is loaded from DRAM. Wait, but if we use shared memory and load input once per block, the input bandwidth is the main bottleneck. Can we reduce it? The input size is essentially fixed. In the kernel: ```cuda for (int ic = 0; ic < 32; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { float in_val = input[...]; float w_val = weight[...]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } ``` The inner loop has 45 iterations. If fully unrolled, that's 45 loads + 45 FMAs. The compiler will probably unroll this with `#pragma unroll`. The address computation and branch overhead are minimal. At 1440 FMAs per thread, with each FMA taking ~1 cycle on H100 (which has very high FP32 throughput), the pure compute time per thread is ~1440 cycles. Here is my plan for the kernel and code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void conv2d_direct_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int H, int W, int OC, int OH, int OW, int KH, int KW, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dil_h, int dil_w ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_outputs = B * OC * OH * OW; if (idx >= total_outputs) return; int tmp = idx; int ow = tmp % OW; tmp /= OW; int oh = tmp % OH; tmp /= OH; int c = tmp % OC; int n = tmp / OC; float acc = bias ? bias[c] : 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) { int h_in = oh * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) { int w_in = ow * stride_w - pad_w + kw * dil_w; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue; float in_val = input[((n * IC + ic) * H + h_in) * W + w_in]; float w_val = weight[((c * IC + ic) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } output[((n * OC + c) * OH + oh) * OW + ow] = acc; } torch::Tensor conv2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t stride_h, int64_t stride_w, int64_t pad_h, int64_t pad_w, int64_t dil_h, int64_t dil_w ) { int B = input.size(0); int IC = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int OC = weight.size(0); int KH = weight.size(2); int KW = weight.size(3); int OH = (H + 2 * pad_h - dil_h * (KH - 1) - 1) / stride_h + 1; int OW = (W + 2 * pad_w - dil_w * (KW - 1) - 1) / stride_w + 1; auto output = torch::empty({B, OC, OH, OW}, input.options()); int total_outputs = B * OC * OH * OW; int threads = 256; int blocks = (total_outputs + threads - 1) / threads; const float* bias_ptr = bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr; conv2d_direct_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias_ptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), B, IC, H, W, OC, OH, OW, KH, KW, stride_h, stride_w, pad_h, pad_w, dil_h, dil_w ); return output; } ''' CPP_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor conv2d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t stride_h, int64_t stride_w, int64_t pad_h, int64_t pad_w, int64_t dil_h, int64_t dil_w ); ''' conv2d_cuda = load_inline( name="conv2d_cuda_v7", cpp_sources=CPP_SOURCE, cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=["conv2d_forward"], ) ``` Wait, I need to double check the weight format. One more optimization: register blocking. If each thread computes multiple output values, we can amortize the loop overhead. But this requires careful thread mapping. With 256 threads per block and total outputs = 134,217,728, we'd have ~524,288 blocks. Each block does very little work. The GPU can schedule many blocks concurrently to hide latency, but there's still launch overhead. If we use 1024 threads per block: ~131,072 blocks. Less overhead. And if each thread computes multiple outputs: fewer blocks, more work per thread. Let's say each thread computes 4 output values (e.g., 4 consecutive pixels in the same channel). Then: - Total "logical threads" = 134,217,728 / 4 = 33,554,432 - With 1024 threads per block: 32,768 blocks This reduces block launch overhead and gives each thread 4*1440 = 5760 FMAs, which is more substantial. For consecutive pixels in the same channel, the inner loop accesses the same weights but different inputs. Since the inputs are consecutive, loads are coalesced. And since each thread handles 4 pixels, it does 4 FMAs per weight load. This is equivalent to 4x unrolling of the spatial loop. The challenge is output writes: each thread writes 4 values to consecutive addresses in the same channel. That's straightforward. Actually, let me see if I can generalize: have each thread compute a "strip" of `VEC` output values. ```cuda // VEC = 4 int base_idx = (blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x) * VEC; for (int v = 0; v < VEC; ++v) { int idx = base_idx + v; if (idx >= total_outputs) break; // compute output[idx] } ``` For `VEC = 4`: - Loop overhead is amortized 4x - Output writes: 4 stores per thread, consecutive - Input loads: for a given (ic, kh, kw), the 4 pixels might have different input positions, but if they are consecutive spatial positions, the input addresses are consecutive (or close) Actually, how do we map `idx` to spatial positions for vectorization? - If we assign `idx` directly to linear output index, then consecutive `idx` values correspond to consecutive output elements. - Output `idx` and `idx+1`: same channel, adjacent spatial pixel - This means the 4 pixels computed by a thread are consecutive in memory. For input, consecutive spatial pixels have input positions that differ by `stride_w` in the W dimension (usually 1). So for `VEC=4`, the inputs for the 4 pixels at position (ic, kh, kw) are at addresses separated by 1 float. These are coalesced within a thread, but not across threads in a warp (a warp handles 32*4 = 128 pixels). Wait, if each thread handles 4 pixels, and a warp has 32 threads, the warp covers 128 pixels. For the input at a specific (ic, kh, kw), threads 0..31 load inputs for pixels 0..127 (with some mapping). These loads are coalesced because they're consecutive. Actually, the input for pixel `i` is at `input[spatial_start + i * stride_w + offset]`. If `stride_w=1`, then pixels 0..127 are loaded from 127 consecutive addresses. A warp loading 128 consecutive floats issues 4 transactions (128 bytes each). Fully coalesced. For weights: all 32 threads in the warp load the SAME weight for the same (c, ic, kh, kw). Uniform load, very efficient. Then each thread does 4 FMAs with the same weight and 4 different inputs. The weight stays in a register, and each thread loads 4 inputs. Wait, that's 4 loads per thread per (ic, kh, kw). Actually, for a strip of 4 pixels, each thread loads 4 input values. A warp loads 128 input values. Memory per warp per iteration: 128 input floats + 1 weight float = 516 bytes FMAs per warp per iteration: 128 * 1 = 128 FMAs = 256 FLOPs AI = 256 / 516 = 0.5 FLOPs/byte. Terrible. But across a block of 1024 threads (256 threads * VEC4): - Memory: 1024 inputs + 1 weight = 4096 + 4 = 4100 bytes - FMAs: 1024 - AI = 2048 / 4100 = 0.5. Still terrible. The issue is that the AI is inherently low for a simple direct kernel. The only way to improve it is to reuse input or weights across iterations, which requires some form of blocking. Since weights are reused across spatial pixels in the same channel, if a block computes many pixels in the same channel, the weight loads are amortized. But in our VEC4 approach, a block of 256 threads computes 1024 pixels in the same channel. So for a given (ic, kh, kw), the weight is loaded once for all 1024 pixels. The AI per block is: - Memory: 1024 inputs + 1 weight = 4096 + 4 = 4100 bytes - FMAs: 1024 * 1 = 1024 (but wait, 1024 pixels * 1 channel, and we do this for all IC*KH*KW) - Compute: 1024 * 32 * 45 = 1,474,560 FMAs = 2,949,120 FLOPs - Memory for all iterations: 32 * 45 * 4100 bytes ≈ 5,904,000 bytes - AI = 2,949,120 / 5,904,000 = 0.5... Wait, that can't be right. For each (ic, kh, kw), we load 1024 different input values. That's 1024 loads. The weights are loaded once but there are 32*45 = 1440 weight loads. Total memory = 1024*1440*4 + 1440*4 = 5,898,240 + 5,760 = 5,904,000 bytes. Compute = 1024*1440*2 = 2,949,120 FLOPs. AI = 0.5. So the kernel is fundamentally memory bound because each input value is used only once per channel (it contributes to one output pixel for each channel, but since we're only computing one channel at a time, each input value is loaded once and used in one FMA). To fix this, we MUST compute multiple channels per block, so that each input value is reused across channels. If a block computes 32 channels and 32 pixels (1024 outputs), then for each (ic, kh, kw): - Input loads: 32 pixels - Weight loads: 32 channels - FMAs: 32 * 32 = 1024 - Memory: 32 + 32 = 64 floats = 256 bytes - For all 1440 positions: 256 * 1440 = 368,640 bytes - Compute: 1024 * 1440 * 2 = 2,949,120 FLOPs - AI = 2,949,120 / 368,640 = 8.0 Better but still below 20. To reach AI > 20, we'd need to compute LOTS of channels per block or lots of pixels per block with many channels. If we compute ALL 64 channels and 256 pixels (16384 outputs): - Per (ic, kh, kw): input = 256, weights = 64 - Memory per iteration: (256 + 64) * 4 = 1280 bytes - FMAs: 256 * 64 = 16384 - For 1440 iterations: 1280 * 1440 = 1,843,200 bytes (but weights might be cached) - Compute: 16384 * 1440 * 2 = 47,185,920 FLOPs - AI = 47,185,920 / 1,843,200 = 25.6 Now we're compute bound! But executing 16384 outputs per block with 1024 threads means 16 outputs per thread. Each thread needs 16 accumulators if each output is a different channel-pixel pair. That's 64 bytes of accumulators. Very manageable. The key is: can we organize threads so that output writes are coalesced? For NCHW format and a block computing 256 pixels × 64 channels: - Linear output index = n * 64 * 512 * 512 + c * 512 * 512 + spatial - We want consecutive threads to write to consecutive memory. - If thread i computes output at linear index i, then for a block, threads 0..1023 compute outputs 0..1023 in linear order. Linear order in NCHW: channels change slowest? No, for tensor index [B, OC, OH, OW], the linear index is: idx = ((n * OC + c) * OH + oh) * OW + ow For consecutive idx: - ow increments by 1 - When ow wraps, oh increments - When oh wraps, c increments - When c wraps, n increments So if we assign thread i to linear output index `block_start + i`: - Threads 0..31 cover ow=0..31, oh=0, c=0, n=0 (assuming block_start aligns) - Thread 32 covers ow=0, oh=1, c=0, n=0 Wait, for OW=512, 32 threads cover less than one row. So: - Threads 0..31: ow=0..31, oh=0, c=0 - Threads 32..63: ow=32..63, oh=0, c=0 - ... - Threads 480..511: ow=480..511, oh=0, c=0 - Thread 512: ow=0, oh=1, c=0 So threads in a warp mostly cover the same row (ow spans 32 consecutive values), and warp 16 covers the next row. For output writes, threads in warp 0 write to row 0, columns 0..31. That's fully coalesced. Warp 1 writes to row 0, columns 32..63. Also coalesced. ... Warp 16 writes to row 1, columns 0..31. Coalesced. Perfect! Each warp's writes are coalesced. For input loads: - Thread idx has spatial position s = block_start_spatial + threadIdx.x (ignoring channel/n) - For a given (ic, kh, kw), input position = s with offsets from the kernel - Since s spans 256 consecutive spatial positions, and consecutive positions have adjacent ow (with oh changing every 512), the input addresses form a mostly contiguous pattern. For a warp of 32 threads all in the same oh row: - They access w_in = ow_start + offset + kw*3 - pad_w - These are 32 consecutive values. Coalesced loads! For a warp spanning two rows (if 32 threads cross the row boundary): - Threads 0..(511-ow_start) access row oh - Remaining threads access row oh+1 - The addresses are in two separate segments, each coalesced. For the general case where spatial positions span many rows: - A warp covers at most 2 rows (since 32 < 512). - So input loads are in at most 2 segments, both coalesced. Great! Input loads are efficient. For weights: - Thread idx corresponds to output with channel c. Different threads may have different c. - For a warp, if threads 0..511 all have c=0, and threads 512..1023 have c=1, then warp 0..15 all load weights for c=0, and warp 16..31 load weights for c=1. - Actually, with 64 channels and 256 pixels per block... wait. Let me recalculate. If we're assigning linear indices: 256 pixels per what? The total block outputs = 256 pixels * 64 channels = 16384. With 1024 threads, each thread handles 16384/1024 = 16 outputs. But we said each thread handles 16 outputs for good register utilization. So each thread's 16 outputs correspond to: - Option 1: 1 pixel for 16 channels - Option 2: 16 pixels for 1 channel - Option 3: 4 pixels for 4 channels For the thread mapping, we need to decide how to divide the 16 outputs per thread. If each thread handles 16 pixels in 1 channel: - 1024 threads / 16 pixels = 64 channel groups - Block covers 64 channels, each with 16 pixels? No, 1024 threads * 16 pixels = 16384 spatial positions, but we said 256 pixels total. This doesn't add up. Let me be more careful: - Total outputs in block = 16384 - Threads = 1024 - Outputs per thread = 16 - We can organize threads as: 1024 threads = 64 channel-groups × 16 pixels per group - Thread (c, p) where c in [0, 63] and p in [0, 15] - Output for thread = channel c, pixel group p, and maybe lane within the pixel group Actually, if we use 1024 threads and each thread computes 16 output values, we need to decide the mapping to the tensor. For coalescing and simplicity: Option A: Thread i computes 16 consecutive pixels in a single channel - Warp of 32 threads: each thread computes 16 pixels in one channel - Total warp outputs = 32 * 16 = 512 pixels in 32 different channels - This is scattered across memory (different channels are far apart). Option B: Thread i computes 16 consecutive channels for a single pixel - Warp of 32 threads: each thread computes 16 channels for 1 pixel - Total warp outputs = 32 pixels × 16 channels = 512 outputs - Memory: scattered across channels for the same pixel.
