Option D: Thread computes a 4×4 tile of outputs: 4 pixels × 4 channels - Warp of 32 threads = 8 such tiles - Total: 8 * 4 * 4 = 128 outputs per warp - Memory pattern: each tile is local, but across the warp, there's some coalescing. This is getting complex for output coalescing with 16 outputs per thread. Let me think differently. **Key insight:** For the inner loop over (ic, kh, kw), we can accumulate into registers. The memory pattern of the output stores only matters once at the end. As long as we have enough compute per thread, the store overhead is small. What if each thread computes ALL outputs for ALL channels for ONE spatial pixel? That's 64 accumulators and 64*1440 = 92160 FMAs. That's a LOT of compute per thread, but the stores are very scattered. Alternatively, what if we use NHWC output format and write at the end? The stores would be contiguous for the same pixel across channels. Actually, we could write the output in NHWC format using my custom kernel, and then transpose back to NCHW. The transpose is a memory-bound operation that takes ~0.15 ms. But if our conv kernel is 10x faster than cuDNN, the transpose overhead is fine. Without knowing if our kernel can be 10x faster, this is risky. Hmm. Let me reverse-engineer cuDNN's performance. For a conv like this: - B=8, IC=32, OC=64, H=W=512 - Effective kernel 9×25 - FLOPs: ~12.4G - On H100, cuDNN FP32 probably achieves 20-50 TFLOPS through optimized GEMM. - Time: 12.4G / 50T = 0.25 ms. A pure custom direct kernel without fancy blocking might achieve: - Memory bandwidth: 3.35 TB/s - Total data: ~768MB minimum - Time: 0.23 ms - But with overhead and imperfect coalescing, maybe ~0.4-0.6 ms. So there's a real chance that even a well-written direct kernel could be SLOWER than cuDNN. **What if cuDNN uses Winograd for this kernel size?** Winograd is typically used for small kernels like 3×3. For a 5×9 kernel with dilation, Winograd might not apply or might not be beneficial. But cuDNN probably uses implicit GEMM, which is its go-to algorithm for most cases. For implicit GEMM, the effective FLOP rate depends on how efficiently the GEMM is executed. With M=2,097,152 (B*H*W), N=64 (OC), K=1440 (IC*KH*KW), this is a very tall-and-thin matrix multiply (M >> K, M >> N). Tall-and-thin GEMMs are notoriously tricky because: 1. They don't fully utilize tensor cores (which prefer square-ish tiles) 2. They have significant overhead from kernel launch and workspace setup 3. The K dimension (1440) is small, limiting parallelism in the reduction dimension For a tall-and-thin GEMM with K=1440, cuBLAS might not achieve great efficiency. But cuDNN has specialized code for conv-to-GEMM transformation. Actually, there IS an opportunity here! If cuDNN uses im2col+GEMM, the im2col step itself is overhead (memory bandwidth). Our direct kernel avoids im2col entirely, computing output directly from input + weights. If cuDNN is memory-bound in the im2col step, our direct kernel might win. For im2col: - Creates a matrix of size M × K = 2,097,152 × 1440 - Each column corresponds to one kernel position (ic, kh, kw) - The im2col operation reads input and scatters into the matrix - Memory traffic: reads 256MB input, writes 2,097,152 × 1440 × 4 = 11.8GB of im2col matrix - Wait, that's huge! 12GB of writes for a 0.25ms operation is impossible. Actually, cuDNN's implicit GEMM doesn't explicitly create the im2col matrix. It uses a "implicit" approach where the input is accessed on-the-fly during the GEMM. So there's no explicit im2col buffer. For implicit GEMM, the tile structure might still have overhead. The matrix multiply is partitioned into tiles, and the input values for each tile are loaded into shared memory or registers. Given the M-dominant shape, the GEMM might launch many thread blocks, each handling a tile of output rows. The weight matrix (K × N = 1440 × 64) is small and can stay in shared memory or registers. Actually, for such a small N and K relative to M, the GEMM can be thought of as many independent dot products of length 1440. Each output value is a dot product between a 1440-length input vector and a 1440-length weight vector. With 134M outputs, there are 134M independent dot products of length 1440. cuDNN probably groups these into tiles where a block of threads computes multiple dot products simultaneously, reusing weights across the block. If a block computes 64 dot products (e.g., 1 output pixel for 64 channels), it loads 1440 weights for each channel (but shared across pixels) and 1440 input values for the pixel. Wait, for 64 channels and 1 pixel: weights = 64 * 1440 = 92160 floats. That's 360 KB, too big for shared memory but fits in L2. Actually, I think cuDNN might use a very wide tile in the channel dimension to maximize weight reuse. For example: - Each block computes 64 channels × 16 pixels = 1024 outputs. - For each of 1440 (ic, kh, kw) positions: - Load 16 input values (for 16 pixels) - Load 64 weight values (for 64 channels) - Compute 16 * 64 = 1024 FMAs - Memory per position: 16 + 64 = 80 floats - AI per position: 1024*2 / (80*4) = 6.4 - For 1440 positions: AI stays at 6.4. cuDNN might process multiple pixels per channel to amortize the weight loads. But fundamentally, for IC*KH*KW=1440 and OC=64, the weight matrix is 1440*64=92160 elements. If a block loads all 92160 weights into shared memory or registers, it can then compute many pixels using only input loads. Can we load all 92160 weights into registers? With 1024 threads, that's 90 floats per thread. That's 360 bytes per thread, or about 90 registers. On H100 with 255 max registers per thread, this IS possible! Input bounding box for 64 pixels in one row: - 64 consecutive ow values - w range: from ow_base - 4 to ow_base + 63 + 24 = ow_base + 83 - Total w values: 88 - h range: 9 values - Bounding box: 9 × 88 = 792 floats = 3168 bytes Shared memory layout: `float in_smem[9][88]` Loading shared memory: ```cuda for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 9 * 88; i += blockDim.x) { int sh_h = i / 88; int sh_w = i % 88; int h = oh_base - 2 + sh_h; // Actually this needs more care ... } ``` Wait, for shared memory indexing: - `sh_h = 0` corresponds to actual `h = oh_base - 2` (if that's >= 0) - `sh_h = 1` corresponds to `h = oh_base`... but actually h takes values oh_base-2, oh_base, oh_base+2, oh_base+4, oh_base+6 for oh_local=0. - If other threads in the warp also have oh_local=0 (since they're all in the same row), then all threads access the same 5 h values. The advantage is simple indexing in shared memory: - For pixel with ow_local = offset, and kernel position kw: w_idx = offset - 4 + kw * 3 - This maps directly to `in_smem[h_idx][w_idx]` where h_idx = kh * 2 (wait, h_idx needs to match the loaded data) Actually, if we load the dense bounding box: - We store at `in_smem[h - (oh_base - 2)][w - (ow_base - 4)]` - Then for pixel with local ow = p, and kernel (kh, kw): - h_in = oh_base - 2 + kh * 2 - w_in = ow_base + p - 4 + kw * 3 - sh_h = h_in - (oh_base - 2) = kh * 2 - sh_w = w_in - (ow_base - 4) = p + kw * 3 - So `in_smem[kh * 2][p + kw * 3]` Wait, but sh_w = p + kw * 3. For p in [0, 63] and kw in [0, 8], sh_w ranges from 0 to 63 + 24 = 87. And our shared memory w dimension is 88 (indices 0..87). Perfect! And sh_h = kh * 2, with kh in [0, 4], so sh_h ranges 0, 2, 4, 6, 8. Our shared memory h dimension is 9, indices 0..8. Perfect! So the shared memory access is: `in_smem[kh * 2][p + kw * 3]` Wait, but this assumes ALL values in the bounding box exist in the image (no out-of-bounds). For edge tiles, some values might be out of bounds and need to be 0. We handle this during loading: ```cuda for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 9 * 88; i += blockDim.x) { int sh_h = i / 88; int sh_w = i % 88; int h = oh_base - 2 + sh_h; int w = ow_base - 4 + sh_w; if (h >= 0 && h < H && w >= 0 && w < W) { in_smem[sh_h][sh_w] = input[...]; } else { in_smem[sh_h][sh_w] = 0.0f; } } ``` This is correct. For the kernel computation: ```cuda int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; int channel = warp_id; // 0..31 int pixel0 = lane; int pixel1 = lane + 32; int c = block_channel_start + channel; float acc0 = bias ? bias[c] : 0.0f; float acc1 = bias ? bias[c] : 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { __syncthreads(); // Load input tile for this ic into shared memory for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < 9 * 88; i += blockDim.x) { // ... load code ... } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 5; ++kh) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 9; ++kw) { float in0 = in_smem[kh * 2][pixel0 + kw * 3]; float in1 = in_smem[kh * 2][pixel1 + kw * 3]; float w = weight[((c * IC + ic) * KH + kh) * KW + kw]; acc0 += in0 * w; acc1 += in1 * w; } } } int global_ow0 = block_ow_base + pixel0; int global_ow1 = block_ow_base + pixel1; // Write outputs output[((n * OC + c) * OH + oh_base) * OW + global_ow0] = acc0; output[((n * OC + c) * OH + oh_base) * OW + global_ow1] = acc1; ``` Wait, output writes: - Thread i in warp c writes to channel c, row oh_base, columns block_ow_base + i and block_ow_base + i + 32 - Warp c writes 64 values in channel c. - But threads in a warp write to non-adjacent columns (gap of 32). Are these coalesced? Adjacent threads (i and i+1) write to columns block_ow_base + i and block_ow_base + i + 1. For acc0, threads 0..31 write to 32 consecutive columns. Fully coalesced as one transaction! For acc1, threads 0..31 write to columns block_ow_base + 32..block_ow_base + 63. Also fully coalesced as one transaction! But each thread writes twice, so a warp issues 2 memory transactions. This is very efficient. Similarly, the weight loads are uniform across the warp (same c, ic, kh, kw for all threads in the warp). So weights are broadcast. And the shared memory loads: - in_smem[kh*2][pixel0 + kw*3] where pixel0 = lane - For fixed kh and kw, threads in a warp access in_smem[kh*2][lane + kw*3] - These access consecutive addresses in the shared memory row (lane + const offset) - No bank conflicts if bank width = 4 bytes and 32 banks, because consecutive addresses map to consecutive banks. - Wait: `in_smem` is `float[9][88]`. Row `kh*2` has 88 floats. Address of element `sh_w` in the row is `row_base + sh_w * 4`. Threads access `sh_w = lane + kw*3`. So threads access `lane + const` which means consecutive threads access consecutive elements. No bank conflicts! This is a very well-designed kernel. Let me commit to this design. Grid and block configuration: - Block: 1024 threads (32 warps) - Each block: 32 channels × 64 pixels (all in one row, oh = oh_base, ow = ow_base..ow_base+63) - Total spatial pixels: B * OH * OW = 8 * 512 * 512 = 2,097,152 - Pixels per block: 64 - Blocks per spatial slice: 2,097,152 / 64 = 32,768 - Total channels: 64 - Channel groups: 64 / 32 = 2 - Total blocks: 32,768 * 2 = 65,536 This is a reasonable number of blocks for an H100 (132 SMs can run many blocks concurrently). Wait, should we increase pixels per block to get fewer blocks? 32,768 blocks per channel group is plenty. H100 has 132 SMs and can queue many blocks. Actually, 65,536 blocks gives 65,536 / 132 ≈ 496 blocks per SM. That's more than enough to keep the GPU busy. And each block has 1024 threads, each thread doing 2 * 32 * 45 = 2880 FMAs. Wait: - 2 pixels per thread - 32 ICs - 45 positions per IC - FMAs = 2 * 32 * 45 = 2880 Plus, each block has tons of shared memory loads, but the compute is substantial. Actually, each warp does: - 64 pixels * 32 ICs * 45 positions = 92,160 FMAs per warp - A block has 32 warps: 2,949,120 FMAs - On H100 at 67 TFLOPS, a block's compute is ~44 microseconds. But with 65,536 blocks, total compute = 12.4 GFLOPs, so total time at 100% utilization = 0.185 ms. The question is: can we get close to 100% utilization? To get 100%, we need to hide all memory latency. The shared memory helps a lot because most accesses are to fast shared memory instead of global memory. Global memory is only used for: 1. Loading the 9*88 = 792 floats of input per IC per block 2. Loading the 32*45 = 1440 weights per IC per block 3. Writing 64 floats * 32 channels = 2048 output floats per block Per IC, global memory traffic: - Input: 792 floats = 3168 bytes - Weights: 1440 floats = 5760 bytes - Total: 8928 bytes - Compute per IC: 64 * 32 * 45 = 92,160 FMAs Time to load 8928 bytes from global memory at 3.35 TB/s: ~2.7 microseconds. Time to compute 92,160 FMAs at 67 TFLOPS: ~2.7 microseconds. Hey! The compute time and memory time are roughly balanced per IC iteration! That's a good sign. With effective overlap and caches, the kernel should achieve decent utilization. For 32 IC iterations: - Total global memory: ~286,000 bytes per block - Total compute: 2,949,120 FMAs - At 100% compute: 44 microseconds - At 100% memory: 86 microseconds (wait, 286KB / 3.35TB/s = 85 microseconds) Hmm, so purely from global memory bandwidth, it would take 85 microseconds if fully serialized. But with caches and overlapping, it might be less. Actually, weight memory for all blocks: 65,536 blocks * 5760 bytes = ~377 MB. But weights are the same for all blocks. So after the first few blocks load weights into L2, subsequent blocks get L2 hits. The actual DRAM weight traffic is about 5760 * 2 = ~11KB (one per channel group)? No, all blocks need weights. With 65,536 blocks all accessing different weight subsets (different channels), the total unique weight traffic is 32 * 64 * 5 * 9 * 4 = 368,640 bytes, which is tiny. Once in L2, all weight loads are L2 hits. So the real global memory traffic is: - Input: 65,536 blocks * 3168 bytes = ~207 MB - Output: 65,536 blocks * 2048 * 4 bytes? Yes. - Total: 207 MB + 512 MB = 719 MB - Time at 3.35 TB/s: 215 microseconds = 0.215 ms At 67 TFLOPS, compute time is 185 microseconds = 0.185 ms. So the kernel is roughly balanced between compute and memory. 0.2-0.25 ms is achievable. Now, how fast is cuDNN? Hard to know without benchmarking, but it's likely in the same range. If cuDNN achieves 0.2 ms, we'd need to achieve significantly less to "win". But wait, maybe cuDNN's algorithm selection takes some overhead, or it doesn't have a perfectly optimized path for this exact shape. Maybe it falls back to a generic im2col+GEMM that has more overhead. For the GEMM approach: - The GEMM itself might be fast, but there's extra format conversion and workspace allocation. - Our direct kernel has no extra allocations, just one kernel launch. Also, cuDNN might not be using Winograd for 5×9 with dilation, since Winograd doesn't work well with dilation. It probably uses implicit GEMM. For implicit GEMM with M=2M, K=1440, N=64, the GEMM is "tall-skinny" which can be suboptimal in cuBLAS. So there's a real possibility that our custom kernel outperforms cuDNN. import os
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

# Preload correct libstdc++ before loading extension
import ctypes
try:
    ctypes.CDLL('/opt/conda/lib/libstdc++.so.6', mode=ctypes.RTLD_GLOBAL)
except Exception:
    pass

CUDA_SOURCE = r'''
#include <cuda_runtime.h>
#include <torch/extension.h>

__global__ void conv2d_fwd_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ in_ptr,
    const float* __restrict__ w_ptr,
    const float* __restrict__ b_ptr,
    float* __restrict__ out_ptr,
    int B, int IC, int H, int W,
    int OC, int OH, int OW,
    int KH, int KW,
    int stride_h, int stride_w,
    int pad_h, int pad_w,
    int dil_h, int dil_w
) {
    int total_out = B * OC * OH * OW;
    int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;
    if (idx >= total_out) return;
    
    int tmp = idx;
    int ow = tmp % OW; tmp /= OW;
    int oh = tmp % OH; tmp /= OH;
    int c  = tmp % OC; tmp /= OC;
    int n  = tmp;
    
    float acc = (b_ptr != nullptr) ? b_ptr[c] : 0.0f;
    
    for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) {
        for (int kh = 0; kh < KH; ++kh) {
            int h_in = oh * stride_h - pad_h + kh * dil_h;
            if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H) continue;
            for (int kw = 0; kw < KW; ++kw) {
                int w_in = ow * stride_w - pad_w + kw * dil_w;
                if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W) continue;
                float in_val = in_ptr[((n * IC + ic) * H + h_in) * W + w_in];
                float w_val = w_ptr[((c * IC + ic) * KH + kh) * KW + kw];
                acc += in_val * w_val;
            }
        }
    }
    out_ptr[((n * OC + c) * OH + oh) * OW + ow] = acc;
}

torch::Tensor conv2d_forward(
    torch::Tensor input,
    torch::Tensor weight,
    c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias,
    int64_t stride_h, int64_t stride_w,
    int64_t pad_h, int64_t pad_w,
    int64_t dil_h, int64_t dil_w
) {
    int B = input.size(0);
    int IC = input.size(1);
    int H = input.size(2);
    int W = input.size(3);
    int OC = weight.size(0);
    int KH = weight.size(2);
    int KW = weight.size(3);
    
    int OH = (H + 2 * pad_h - dil_h * (KH - 1) - 1) / stride_h + 1;
    int OW = (W + 2 * pad_w - dil_w * (KW - 1) - 1) / stride_w + 1;
    
    auto output = torch::empty({B, OC, OH, OW}, input.options());
    
    int total_out = B * OC * OH * OW;
    const int threads = 256;
    int blocks = (total_out + threads - 1) / threads;
    
    const float* bias_ptr = bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr;
    
    conv2d_fwd_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(
        input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias_ptr, output.data_ptr<float>(),
        B, IC, H, W, OC, OH, OW, KH, KW,
        (int)stride_h, (int)stride_w, (int)pad_h, (int)pad_w, (int)dil_h, (int)dil_w
    );
    
    return output;
}
'''

CPP_SOURCE = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>

torch::Tensor conv2d_forward(
    torch::Tensor input,
    torch::Tensor weight,
    c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias,
    int64_t stride_h, int64_t stride_w,
    int64_t pad_h, int64_t pad_w,
    int64_t dil_h, int64_t dil_w
);
'''

conv2d_cuda = load_inline(
    name="conv2d_cuda_v10",
    cpp_sources=CPP_SOURCE,
    cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE,
    functions=["conv2d_forward"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "-use_fast_math"],
    extra_cflags=["-O3", "-D_GLIBCXX_USE_CXX11_ABI=0"],
)

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=(0,0), dilation=(1,1), bias=False):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)
    
    def forward(self, x):
        return conv2d_cuda.conv2d_forward(
            x, self.conv2d.weight, self.conv2d.bias,
            self.conv2d.stride[0] if isinstance(self.conv2d.stride, tuple) else self.conv2d.stride,
            self.conv2d.stride[1] if isinstance(self.conv2d.stride, tuple) else self.conv2d.stride,
            self.conv2d.padding[0], self.conv2d.padding[1],
            self.conv2d.dilation[0], self.conv2d.dilation[1]
        )
